U.S. News: Recovery In Building Is Forecast For 2011
By A.D. Pruitt
425 words
29 October 2010
The Wall Street Journal
J
A2
English
(Copyright (c) 2010, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

The nation's construction industry, virtually on life support during the economic downturn, will begin a slow recovery next year, according to a forecast set to be released Friday.

Next year, the value of new projects that start construction is expected to climb to $445.5 billion, an 8% rise from this year when that figure hit a post-recession low, according to the closely watched McGraw-Hill Construction forecast.

New development of single-family houses, apartment buildings and commercial properties is expected to increase. But there will be less building of new highways, bridges and other public works as federal stimulus money dries up, according to the forecast.

Even with an increase next year, construction activity will still be far off the boom-year peak it hit in 2006 when there were $689.3 billion in construction starts, according to McGraw-Hill.

McGraw-Hill last year initially forecasted a gain of 11% for 2010, but that projection proved to be overly optimistic. Construction starts are estimated to decline 2% from 2009 as a recovery in the housing industry stalled in the middle of the year, credit remained sparse and municipal budget deficits widened.

"State and local governments saw their fiscal health erode to a greater extent than previously thought," said Robert Murray, vice president of economic affairs at McGraw-Hill Construction, part of New York-based McGraw-Hill Cos. McGraw-Hill expects the U.S. economy will grow 2.5% in 2011.

Among specific sectors, single-family housing should see the strongest rebound in 2011, with $126.7 billion in construction starts, a 27% boost, according to the forecast. But that projection assumes mortgage rates remain low, job growth improves and a resolution is found to the foreclosure documentation mess. The single-family estimate is 60% below the peak in 2005, underscoring how far builders have curtailed construction following the housing boom.

New construction of multifamily housing is expected to rise 24% to $23.6 billion next year, extending gains from 2010 as a growing number of renters enter the market amid the housing crisis. A 1% decline is expected for public-works construction, which should come in at $122.3 billion as a lack of federal stimulus money and budget woes of state and local governments put a lid on new projects.

Meantime, commercial buildings -- which includes offices, stores and hotels -- will improve with a 16% gain to $44.9 billion.

Real Estate
Recovery in Building Is Forecast for 2011
By A.D. Pruitt
492 words
29 October 2010
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2010 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

The nation's construction industry, virtually on life support during the economic downturn, will begin a slow recovery next year, according to a forecast set to be released Friday.

Next year, the value of new projects that start construction is expected to climb to $445.5 billion, an 8% rise from this year when that figure hit a post-recession low, according to the closely watched McGraw-Hill Construction forecast.

New development of single-family houses, apartment buildings and commercial properties is expected to increase. But there will be less building of new highways, bridges and other public works as federal stimulus money dries up, according to the forecast.

Even with an increase next year, construction activity will still be far off the boom-year peak it hit in 2006 when there were $689.3 billion in construction starts, according to McGraw-Hill.

McGraw-Hill last year initially forecasted a gain of 11% for 2010, but that projection proved to be overly optimistic. Construction starts are estimated to decline 2% from 2009 as a recovery in the housing industry stalled in the middle of the year, credit remained sparse and municipal budget deficits widened.

"State and local governments saw their fiscal health erode to a greater extent than previously thought," said Robert Murray, vice president of economic affairs at McGraw-Hill Construction, part of New York-based McGraw-Hill Cos.

McGraw-Hill expects the U.S. economy will grow 2.5% in 2011. "The economy will continue to struggle in 2011 although avoid another recession," Mr. Murray said.

Among specific sectors, single-family housing should see the strongest rebound in 2011, with $126.7 billion in construction starts, a 27% boost, according to the forecast. But that projection assumes mortgage rates remain low, job growth improves and a resolution is found to the foreclosure documentation mess. The single-family estimate is 60% below the peak in 2005, underscoring how far builders have curtailed construction following the housing boom.

New construction of multifamily housing is expected to rise 24% to $23.6 billion next year, extending gains from 2010 as a growing number of renters enter the market amid the housing crisis. A 1% decline is expected for public-works construction, which should come in at $122.3 billion as a lack of federal stimulus money and budget woes of state and local governments put a lid on new projects.

Meantime, commercial buildings—which includes offices, stores, hotels and warehouses—will improve with a 16% gain to $44.9 billion. But that comes after falling 17% in 2010 and 43% in 2009. In 2010, construction starts hit a 50-year low.

McGraw-Hill projects that construction starts of manufacturing buildings will increase 9% as the drop in value of the U.S. dollar increases demand for U.S. goods.

Property: Designers Enlisted in Sales Wars
By Shelly Banjo
610 words
28 October 2010
The Wall Street Journal
J
A22
English
(Copyright (c) 2010, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

Filmmakers Chandra and Pierson Silver spent a year looking to buy their first apartment in Fort Greene, Brooklyn. They took their time in what they felt was a buyer's market, until they walked into a designer model apartment at the Solis on Clermont Avenue.

"Buying your first apartment is emotional, so when we saw the way the apartment was set up and it was perfect, we decided to buy it," Ms. Silver says. "We loved the layout so much we even took most of the furniture."

As the real-estate market in New York City slowly rebounds, developers are getting even more aggressive in pushing sales, particularly of new developments that were stalled during the slowdown and are just now coming online.

To that end, developers at such buildings as the Aldyn and Setai Fifth Avenue in Manhattan and the Solis and be@schermerhorn apartments in Brooklyn are spending millions of dollars for interior decorators and fashion designers to turn otherwise plain-vanilla model apartments into fashionable residences. They hope using opulent furniture and high fashion will help potential buyers envision the spaces as their own, and ultimately boost sales.

Tuesday night, nearly 600 potential buyers came to the Manhattan House on the Upper East Side as the building unveiled a set of model apartments by interior designers James "Ford" Huniford, Celerie Kemble and Rita Konig called the Modern Collection, a portfolio of residences designed in response to growing consumer demand for larger spaces.

The designers were given three months and hundreds of thousands of dollars to design and build out each apartment, which range from one- to four-bedroom units. The work was to have a bent toward creating more space for eventual residents. But other than that and their spending budget, the designers say they were free to search antique shops and paint stores for the makings of distinctive looks for the apartments.

"In this market it's all about market differentiation and connecting people with the space," says Ms. Kemble, who designed a 1,861-square-foot, three-bedroom apartment with glazed black lacquer walls, using vintage textiles. "It's not just about looking at the floor plan, it's whether or not I can see myself living here."

An added bonus, she says, is the droves of new clients who call her after the model showings.

Designed in 1950 by architect Gordon Bunshaft and now holding landmark status, the Manhattan House was converted into luxury condominiums in 2008. As part of the $1.1 billion conversion, one of the most expensive in the city's history, developer O'Connor Capital Partners added amenities such as a rooftop spa and fitness club. Even so, "the apartments barely moved until the spring of 2009," says partner Brian Fallon.

In 2009, O'Connor switched marketing firms to Corcoran Sunshine Marketing Group and plowed more than $2 million into a set of designer model apartments.

Since then, 12 model units have been sold, as well as 20 others that are of similar layouts to the model apartments, representing more than $85 million in sales. Currently, O'Connor has sold about 40% of the available units in Manhattan House.

"Most buyers don't have the skill set to envision what their apartments could look like so if we can do it for them, it removes the doubt and suddenly they say, 'I could see myself in that space," says Mr. Fallon, who employed a similar approach at the InterContinental Boston hotel. "For a small investment, this has been a remarkable sales tool."

New York Real Estate
Designers Enlisted in Sales Wars
By Shelly Banjo
615 words
28 October 2010
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2010 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

In 2009, O'Connor switched marketing firms to Corcoran Sunshine Marketing Group and plowed more than $2 million into a set of designer model apartments.

The Property Report
Construction in New York? Skip It --- With Market Still Troubled, Big Apple Office Builders Sit on Their Hands
By A.D. Pruitt
741 words
27 October 2010
The Wall Street Journal
J
C8
English
(Copyright (c) 2010, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

Mumbai will add 30 million square feet of new office space in 2010 and 2011. Dubai will add 20 million square feet, and Shanghai 14 million during the same period.

But after a Times Square building is completed next month, the total space in office buildings in New York City isn't expected to grow at all between then and the end of 2011.

While office-building construction is on a tear in emerging markets, it has largely halted in New York and most of the other traditional financial centers of the U.S. Chicago, Los Angeles and San Francisco will be delivering just 120,000 square feet, 695,000 square feet and 515,000 square feet respectively in 2010 and 2011, according to a recent report by Jones Lang LaSalle.

Europe is doing better. London is expected to add 5.8 million square feet in the two-year period. Developers in Paris are slated to deliver 12 million square feet, according to Jones Lang LaSalle. The slowdown in the U.S., of course, is because the weak economy and the credit crunch have made most development financially unfeasible. But as the world economy rebounds and competition heats up among financial centers, the availability of modern office space will play a part in determining winners and losers.

"I think it's dangerous, because people need facilities, and there is no place to go," says New York's Larry Silverstein, the developer of three office towers at the World Trade Center site. Construction on all three Silverstein towers is under way, but only Tower 4 is currently being built to completion. Tower 3 will get a government financial backstop once Mr. Silverstein meets certain triggers; Tower 2 will be built to street level, and its construction will be based on market demand.

Mr. Silverstein notes that 60% of the buildings in New York City are more than 60 years old. "For the most part, I think it serves as a depressant not to have first-class facilities available at the time they want to move to them," he says.

Modern space is particularly important for financial-services firms that need high ceilings, raised floors, column-free space and the latest broadband and telecommunications infrastructure. Newer buildings also tend to be more energy efficient and can be important tools for attracting and retaining top staff.

Mr. Silverstein is able to build thanks largely to subsidies and the availability of insurance funds. But most developers and real-estate investors in the U.S. say they don't expect construction lending to resume at least for another three years because rents don't justify it. In New York, gross rents would have to increase roughly 30% to justify new unsubsidized construction, experts say.

At the same time, buildings are selling for less than it would cost to replace them. The last large-scale office tower set for completion in Manhattan before at least 2012 is 11 Times Square, a 40-story, 1.1-million-square-foot skyscraper that will be officially completed next month. Another new building of that magnitude isn't likely to be able to get a construction loan until gross rents increase about $30 from roughly $82 a square foot currently, say developers and investors.

"You won't see much in the way of speculative development. The financing is not there," says Mitchell Hersh, chief executive of Mack-Cali Realty Corp., New Jersey's largest office landlord.

Developers say there is a positive side to the dearth of construction in the U.S.: The lack of new supply has cushioned the office market from even harsher blows from the economic downturn.

In the early 1990s, the market collapsed under tens of millions of square feet of office space that was delivered during a speculative building boom. During the last cycle, while investors bid up the price of existing buildings to exorbitant levels, developers restrained themselves when it came to building new properties. "It's discouraging and a bad barometer for . . . future growth that we're not seeing speculative development," says Peter Riguardi, president of the New York region at Jones Lang LaSalle.

But he adds, "It's actually very prudent given where we are in the markets right now."

Forecast Sees Rise in Building
By Shelly Banjo
646 words
27 October 2010
The Wall Street Journal
J
A21
English
(Copyright (c) 2010, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

More than 700 stalled building sites coupled with three years of declines in spending have rocked the city's wobbly construction market, but a new report expects the industry to start to rebound.

Construction spending fell 12% from last year and 23% from 2008 to $23.7 billion, according to a report by the New York Building Congress, an alliance of business, government and labor groups.

The spending declines come in nearly every building category and represent a bleak spot in an otherwise recovering New York real-estate market, which has seen a rebound in sales and rentals over the past few quarters that has defied national housing trends.

While the Building Congress forecasts construction spending and growth to pick up through 2012, other industry experts aren't sure the rebound will come so quickly.

"There are literally zero square feet coming online in 2011 and 2012," says Victor Calanog, director of research at Reiss Inc., a real-estate data company. "What's coming online will have opened its doors this year with new building completely frozen."

The report is based on information from the state Department of Labor, public capital budgets and plans, as well as the consulting firm Urbanomics and data provider McGraw-Hill Construction Dodge.

The construction industry is relying on city and state government funding to pick up the slack left by private developers who helped fuel the latest building boom: Based on a review of agency budgets and projected commitments, the Building Congress said government spending is expected to increase to $15.2 billion in 2011 and $18.6 billion in 2012 from $14.6 billion this year.

The bulk of the projected jump in construction spending in 2012, though, comes from Metropolitan Transportation Authority projects that have no dedicated funding, the report said.

While the MTA's planned $3.5 billion in construction spending for 2011 is secure, the agency has yet to announce the additional funding to finance the full $7.6 billion proposed in 2012 through its current capital plan, which runs through 2014. The agency will be working with the city and state to try to fill that gap, a spokesman said.

In 2010, government construction spending represented 62% of all New York City construction dollars, marking the highest proportion of public spending since 1996.

The heavy reliance on government spending comes with its own risks, says Robert Murray, chief economist at McGraw-Hill Construction.

"The government sector is going to be critical for near-term support for the construction industry," Mr. Murray says, "but the questions is, as cities grapple with rising budgets and rein in spending, will that money be there?"

Meanwhile, the residential sector remains in a deep slump, reporting $1.6 billion in spending in 2010, down from $6.2 billion in 2008.

Likewise, the commercial construction is being kept afloat by large projects such as the rebuilding of the World Trade Center, the $800 million Barclays Arena at Atlantic Yards and $850 million Madison Square Garden redevelopment.

Slowing growth are the more than 700 stalled construction sites across New York City, 46% of which are in Brooklyn, according to the Department of Buildings, which updates a database of stalled projects weekly.

Last year, the city put in place a new law that allows property owners to extend their building permits from 12 months to four years while projects remain on hold, provided they meet certain safety requirements.

"The changes are good but by itself, it isn't enough to get these projects going," says Richard Anderson, president of the Building Congress. "We're in talks with the city to come up with a more comprehensive program; otherwise it will be saddled with projects for years to come that will be unsightly, dangerous and a real headache."

The Property Report
Plots & Ploys / Breaking News From WSJ.com's Developments Blog
541 words
27 October 2010
The Wall Street Journal
J
C6
English
(Copyright (c) 2010, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

Louisville Deal

Pushes Limits

---

Price Tag of $115 Million

Makes National City Tower

Priciest Purchase Since '02

KBS Realty Advisors Cos., a real-estate investment company that has been making some aggressive office-building acquisitions, is close to yet another bet. An untraded real-estate investment trust managed by Newport Beach, Calif.,-based KBS has agreed to pay about $115 million in cash for the National City Tower in Louisville, Ky., according to a person familiar with the deal.

The building last sold in 2005 for about $95 million, according to CoStar.

If the transaction closes, it would be the highest overall price paid for a Louisville office building since 2004, when the Aegon Center sold for $128.5 million, or about $169 a square foot, according to Real Capital Analytics.

The seller is an affiliate of Meridian Investments Inc., according to people familiar with the deal. Meridian officials didn't return calls seeking comment.

-- Maura Webber Sadovi

It Ain't Like the '90s

Selling foreclosed real estate for the FDIC was supposed to be a bonanza for the brokerages such as CB Richard Ellis Group Inc. and Cushman & Wakefield Inc. that contracted to do so during the recession.

But so far, those contracts, while lucrative, aren't anything close to the windfalls reaped in the early 1990s from managing and selling seized properties for the Federal Deposit Insurance Corp. and the Resolution Trust Corp. That is mostly because, this time around, the FDIC has focused more on selling distressed loan portfolios, as the failed banks of this recession largely abstained from foreclosing on commercial property.

In 2008, 2009 and the first two-thirds of this year, the FDIC sold, mostly through contractors, $257.3 million worth of commercial real estate it inherited from failed banks, FDIC data show. More remains on the books: As of Aug. 31, the FDIC still had $630.5 million of foreclosed commercial property in its possession. What's more, in this year's first eight months, the FDIC sold $255.7 million of foreclosed homes and apartment buildings. It has another $477 million of residential properties still on its hands.

All that is nothing compared to the RTC's heyday. From 1990 to 1996, the FDIC and the RTC handled nearly $24 billion of foreclosed real estate inherited from failed banks and thrifts.

-- Kris Hudson

Pre-Emptive Pay Rise

It is good to be wanted, as Donald Wood, president and chief executive of shopping center landlord Federal Realty Investment Trust, learned this month.

Mr. Wood was a favored candidate as a committee of General Growth Properties Inc.'s board searched for the mall owner's next CEO, people familiar with the talks said.

Once Federal's board was informed that its man might depart, it acted. In a filing with the Securities and Exchange Commission made Oct. 13, Federal outlined a generous increase in Mr. Wood's pay aimed at rewarding him for Federal's strong performance and countering "the active recruiting for Mr. Woods' services in the marketplace."

Mr. Wood and Federal representatives didn't return calls seeking comment.

-- Kris Hudson

The Property Report
In Brief
170 words
27 October 2010
The Wall Street Journal
J
C9
English
(Copyright (c) 2010, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

Property Prices

Slide in Survey

An index compiled by Moody's Investors Service found that commercial property prices have started to slide again. The Moody's/REAL Commercial Property Price Index was 105.37 in August, down 3.3% from July. Moody's said the latest reading is the lowest since early 2002. Not all sectors of real estate had declines. Moody's said prices were down for retail and industrial properties but were up for office buildings and apartments.

CBRE, Jones Lang

See Revenue Rise

Revenue for commercial-brokerage firm CB Richard Ellis Group Inc. hit $1.3 billion in the third quarter, a 24% increase from the same period in 2009, the company said Tuesday. Another brokerage, Jones Lang LaSalle Inc., reported third-quarter revenue of $708 million, an increase of 20% in local currency from the third quarter of 2009. Will Marks, an analyst for JMP Securities, said the increase reflects "pent-up demand."

Real Estate
CBRE, Jones Lang See Revenue Rise
By Anton Troianovski
336 words
27 October 2010
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2010 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

While the commercial-real-estate market is experiencing a rocky recovery, profits for some commercial-brokerage firms have risen, as the transaction volume has come back from a near standstill in 2009.

Revenue for commercial-brokerage firm CB Richard Ellis Group Inc. hit $1.3 billion in the third quarter, a 24% increase from the same period in 2009, the company said Tuesday. Another brokerage, Jones Lang LaSalle Inc., reported third-quarter revenue of $708 million, an increase of 20% in local currency from the third quarter of 2009. Will Marks, an analyst for JMP Securities, said the increase in revenue reflects "pent-up demand."

While both firms perform a variety of real-estate-related services, such as property management and investment management, some of their strongest performance came in their bread-and-butter business lines, selling and leasing office buildings, warehouses, retail space and other kinds of commercial property. CB Richard Ellis said its property-sales revenue in the Americas increased 69% in the third quarter compared with a year ago, while leasing revenue was up 36%. Jones Lang LaSalle reported a 38% increase in leasing revenue and a 127% rise in capital markets and hotels revenue in the Americas.

"Our clients around the world are relatively optimistic about the long term," said CB Richard Ellis Chief Executive Brett White. "Many of them were on the sidelines the last two and a half years, and are now getting back in the business of doing business."

In particular, Mr. White said, businesses are signing long-term leases of seven years or more after several years of being skittish about committing to anything at all. Both Mr. White and Jones Lang LaSalle Chief Executive Colin Dyer said they saw rental rates bottoming in major cities around the world.

"Leasing markets now in big cities are virtually all bottoming or have bottomed," Mr. Dyer said. "That's driving a new level of tension on the leasing side."

Dow Jones & Company, Inc.

Commercial Real Estate
Itching for a Deal, Vornado Tweaks Its Tastes; Real-Estate Investment Trust Invests in a J.C. Penney Stake After Being Disappointed by Offerings in the Property Realm
By Anton Troianovski
909 words
27 October 2010
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2010 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

Two years ago, Vornado Realty Trust Chairman Steven Roth, one of the biggest deal makers in the commercial-real-estate industry, suggested that he wanted to get out of the business of making complex, one-time investments in other companies.

His resolve didn't last long. This month, Vornado announced its biggest acquisition since 2007: a stake in retail chain J.C. Penney Co.

Vornado's $600 million investment in J.C. Penney, which was made alongside hedge fund Pershing Square Capital Management, is further proof that these are frustrating times for investors like Mr. Roth who have built up buying power to take advantage of distressed opportunities in today's turbulent market but are finding little to acquire. With interest rates low and banks avoiding fire sales, very few of the prime assets that buyers like Mr. Roth covet are coming on the market. When they do, bidding wars ensue.

Accumulating more than 26% of J.C. Penney's shares is a way for Mr. Roth and William Ackman, head of Pershing, to buy real estate indirectly. They are likely hoping investor pessimism about retailers has masked the higher value of the more than 1,000 stores owned and leased by Penney.

"If you look at the math and you look at the investment, I think you'll agree it's a pretty terrific investment," Mr. Roth said in a brief interview about the Penney deal.

The investment follows a decade in which Vornado, long a Wall Street darling, has lurched from one strategy to another amid the market's gyrations, while suffering several missteps. In June 2006, as the bubble expanded, Mr. Roth said that "in this environment over the last 10 years, every single deal we didn't do was a mistake." Vornado went on to buy skyscrapers in San Francisco and New York for $1.8 billion.

Three years later, in 2009, Mr. Roth acknowledged the company made the mistake of not selling "everything noncore." "We hesitated as the knife started dropping, and now it's too late," he said in a letter to shareholders.

Vornado is at risk of losing a $103 million investment in a Springfield, Va., shopping mall, with the lender seeking to sell the defaulted loan through Mission Capital Advisors to a buyer who would likely seek to foreclose, according to people familiar with the matter. High-profile development projects in New York and Boston have been hobbled by the economy and political obstacles.

A $1.5 billion foray into mezzanine lending produced "unacceptable results," Mr. Roth acknowledged earlier this year.

At the same time, the Penney investment is drawing criticism from analysts who say the deal won't present long-term value for shareholders if Vornado simply makes money on a rising stock price.

"If you think about the long-term growth potential that Vornado has, it's in their real estate," said Alexander Goldfarb, who follows Vornado for Sandler O'Neill & Partners LP. "When you're investing in stock, you're just investing in the vagaries of the market."

Mr. Roth defended the Penney investment, noting his extensive background in retail. "For me to invest in a large retailer—I've done it my whole life, and so this is a perfectly natural investment," he said.

Mr. Roth has been buying strapped retailers for their real-estate portfolios since the early 1980s, when he took control of a struggling New Jersey discount retailer named Two Guys From Harrison. Most famously, Mr. Roth in the early 1990s bought into Alexander's Inc., a bankrupt retailer that owned Manhattan real estate. The deal paid off years later, when the headquarters of financial-data provider Bloomberg LP was built on Alexander's land.

In 1997, Vornado got a foothold in the Manhattan office market by buying stakes in seven office buildings for $700 million. But even as he has amassed one of the country's largest portfolios of real estate, Mr. Roth hasn't been able to resist investing in retailers themselves. In the 2000s, Vornado made money buying stock in McDonald's Corp., Sears Holdings Corp. and Sears Canada Inc. It still holds a private-equity stake in Toys 'R' Us.

But in April 2008, Mr. Roth suggested in a letter to shareholders that he would seek to wean the company off of "one-time" deals like investments in McDonald's and Sears Holdings because he didn't believe Wall Street was giving Vornado enough credit for them. "These investments seem to attract too much attention, taking away focus from our much, much larger, strong core business," Mr. Roth wrote. "The order of the day is to simplify and prune."

Penney could be a good real-estate play because investors may be undervaluing Penney's real-estate interests. Vornado could help the retailer profit from its stores by subletting space leased at below-market rates or even redeveloping Penney-owned property in the best locations, analysts and investors say. "It says something about how the property markets are currently priced when they're pursuing a retailer in lieu of physical real-estate assets," said Ian Goltra, who manages portfolios of real-estate stocks for Forward Funds.

Commercial Real Estate
Construction in New York? Skip It. With Market Still Troubled, Big Apple Office Builders Sit on Their Hands
By A.D. Pruitt
756 words
27 October 2010
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2010 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

Mumbai will add 30 million square feet of new office space in 2010 and 2011. Dubai will add 20 million square feet, and Shanghai 14 million during the same period.

But after a Times Square building is completed next month, the total space in office buildings in New York City isn't expected to grow at all between then and the end of 2011.

While office-building construction is on a tear in emerging markets, it has largely halted in New York and most of the other traditional financial centers of the U.S. Chicago, Los Angeles and San Francisco will be delivering just 120,000 square feet, 695,000 square feet and 515,000 square feet respectively in 2010 and 2011, according to a recent report by Jones Lang LaSalle.

Europe is doing better. London is expected to add 5.8 million square feet in the two-year period. Developers in Paris are slated to deliver 12 million square feet, according to Jones Lang LaSalle. The slowdown in the U.S., of course, is because the weak economy and the credit crunch have made most development financially unfeasible. But as the world economy rebounds and competition heats up among financial centers, the availability of modern office space will play a part in determining winners and losers.

"I think it's dangerous, because people need facilities, and there is no place to go," says New York's Larry Silverstein, the developer of three office towers at the World Trade Center site. Construction on all three Silverstein towers is under way, but only Tower 4 is currently being built to completion. Tower 3 will get a government financial backstop once Mr. Silverstein meets certain triggers; Tower 2 will be built to street level, and its construction will be based on market demand.

Mr. Silverstein notes that 60% of the buildings in New York City are more than 60 years old. "For the most part, I think it serves as a depressant not to have first-class facilities available at the time they want to move to them," he says.

Modern space is particularly important for financial-services firms that need high ceilings, raised floors, column-free space and the latest broadband and telecommunications infrastructure. Newer buildings also tend to be more energy efficient and can be important tools for attracting and retaining top staff.

Mr. Silverstein is able to build thanks largely to subsidies and the availability of insurance funds. But most developers and real-estate investors in the U.S. say they don't expect construction lending to resume at least for another three years because rents don't justify it. In New York, gross rents would have to increase roughly 30% to justify new unsubsidized construction, experts say.

At the same time, buildings are selling for less than it would cost to replace them. The last large-scale office tower set for completion in Manhattan before at least 2012 is 11 Times Square, a 40-story, 1.1-million-square-foot skyscraper that will be officially completed next month. Another new building of that magnitude isn't likely to be able to get a construction loan until gross rents increase about $30 from roughly $82 a square foot currently, say developers and investors.

"You won't see much in the way of speculative development. The financing is not there," says Mitchell Hersh, chief executive of Mack-Cali Realty Corp., New Jersey's largest office landlord. On the other hand, some companies are interested in "build-to-suit" properties, or buildings custom-made for one large tenant, he says.

Developers say there is a positive side to the dearth of construction in the U.S.: The lack of new supply has cushioned the office market from even harsher blows from the economic downturn.

In the early 1990s, the market collapsed under tens of millions of square feet of office space that was delivered during a speculative building boom. During the last cycle, while investors bid up the price of existing buildings to exorbitant levels, developers restrained themselves when it came to building new properties. "It's discouraging and a bad barometer for … future growth that we're not seeing speculative development," says Peter Riguardi, president of the New York region at Jones Lang LaSalle.

But he adds, "It's actually very prudent given where we are in the markets right now."

Dow Jones & Company, Inc.

Plots & Ploys
Louisville Deal Pushes Limits; Price Tag of $115 Million Makes National City Tower Priciest Purchase Since '04
728 words
27 October 2010
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2010 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

KBS Realty Advisors Cos., a real-estate investment company that has been making some aggressive office-building acquisitions, is close to yet another bet. An untraded real-estate investment trust managed by Newport Beach, Calif.,-based KBS has agreed to pay about $115 million in cash for the National City Tower in Louisville, Ky., according to a person familiar with the deal.

The 38-story 712,533-square-foot building in Louisville's central business district was built in 1972 and is 94% leased, according to CoStar Group Inc., a real-estate research firm. The building last sold in 2005 for about $95 million, according to CoStar.

If the transaction closes, it would be the highest overall price paid for a Louisville office building since 2004, when the Aegon Center sold for $128.5 million, or about $169 a square foot, according to Real Capital Analytics. KBS has made other bold moves. Over the summer, KBS REIT II acquired a trophy office building at 300 North LaSalle in Chicago for about $655 million, or about $500 a square foot, the most ever paid for a Chicago office building on a square-foot basis, according to Real Capital.

The seller is an affiliate of Meridian Investments Inc., according to people familiar with the deal. Meridian officials didn't return calls seeking comment.

Maura Webber Sadovi

It Ain't Like the '90s

Selling foreclosed real estate for the FDIC was supposed to be a bonanza for the brokerages such as CB Richard Ellis Group Inc. and Cushman & Wakefield Inc. that contracted to do so during the recession.

But so far, those contracts, while lucrative, aren't anything close to the windfalls reaped in the early 1990s from managing and selling seized properties for the Federal Deposit Insurance Corp. and the Resolution Trust Corp. That is mostly because, this time around, the FDIC has focused more on selling distressed loan portfolios, as the failed banks of this recession largely abstained from foreclosing on commercial property.

In 2008, 2009 and the first two-thirds of this year, the FDIC sold, mostly through contractors, $257.3 million worth of commercial real estate it inherited from failed banks, FDIC data show. More remains on the books: As of Aug. 31, the FDIC still had $630.5 million of foreclosed commercial property in its possession. What's more, in this year's first eight months, the FDIC sold $255.7 million of foreclosed homes and apartment buildings. It has another $477 million of residential properties still on its hands.

All that is nothing compared to the RTC's heyday. From 1990 to 1996, the FDIC and the RTC handled nearly $24 billion of foreclosed real estate inherited from failed banks and thrifts.

Kris Hudson

Pre-emptive Pay Hike

It's good to be wanted, as Donald Wood, president and chief executive of shopping center landlord Federal Realty Investment Trust, learned this month.

Mr. Wood was a favored candidate as a committee of General Growth Properties Inc.'s board searched for the mall owner's next CEO, people familiar with the talks said. He went "well into the process" with the board committee, conducting several interviews, these people said.

Once Federal's board was informed that its man might depart, it acted. In a filing with the Securities and Exchange Commission made Oct. 13, Federal outlined a generous increase in Mr. Wood's pay aimed at rewarding him for Federal's strong performance and countering "the active recruiting for Mr. Woods' services in the marketplace."

Mr. Wood's salary was boosted to $850,000 from $700,000. His maximum bonus jumped to 150% of his salary from 100%. His potential equity bonus in Federal's long-term incentive award program over three years rose to $4 million from $2 million. And he received a grant of $5 million worth of Federal stock in exchange for his agreement that, if he leaves Federal, he won't join any competing real-estate company for a year.

General Growth is now finalizing a pact for Vornado Realty Trustexecutive Sandeep Mathrani to become its next CEO. Mr. Wood and Federal representatives didn't return calls seeking comment. Mr. Mathrani declined to comment through a Vornado representative.

U.S. News: Housing Gloom Deepens --- Home Sales Rise, but Economists Don't See Prices Bottoming Till Late '11 or '12
By Nick Timiraos
989 words
26 October 2010
The Wall Street Journal
J
A3
English
(Copyright (c) 2010, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

Home sales picked up in September, but the long-term picture for housing is growing grimmer, say analysts and economists who are pushing back forecasts for a housing recovery.

Earlier this year, the housing market appeared poised for a turnaround. Federal tax credits for buyers spurred a flurry of activity, and the economy was adding jobs. That led some economists to forecast housing would hit bottom and begin to recover this year.

Now, some economists don't see a recovery until late next year or early 2012. "In most markets, the tide seems to be going back out," said Stan Humphries, chief economist at Zillow.com, a real-estate site. "The momentum is easing."

Adding to the mounting worries is the foreclosure crisis. Some banks suspended sales of foreclosed homes late last month to address questions about the integrity of the foreclosure process. If a substantial part of the market freezes for some weeks, that could further crimp sales.

On Monday, the National Association of Realtors said sales of previously owned homes ran at a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 4.53 million in September. While that was up 10% from August, the overall level of sales remains weak, and economists believe sales going forward could fall again.

Indeed, half of the 109 economists and housing analysts polled this month by MacroMarkets LLC, a housing-futures firm, expect home prices to bottom next year, but half don't expect a rebound until 2012.

The growing pessimism is attributed partly to rising inventory in many markets, a trend that doesn't bode well for prices. The Wall Street Journal's latest quarterly survey of housing-market conditions in 28 major metropolitan areas found inventories of unsold homes were up in 19 markets at the end of the third quarter, compared with a year ago, with especially large increases in San Diego, Los Angeles and Sacramento.

"We'll see some additional price declines," said David Berson, chief economist at PMI Group Inc., a mortgage-insurance company in Walnut Creek, Calif. "The gains we've seen can't be sustained given the current supply situation."

The housing market typically plays a big role as the economy exits from recession, with a rebound in new home building sparking demand for construction workers and building supplies. "We're clearly not getting that this time," said MF Global economist James O'Sullivan. It's a major reason, he said, that the recovery that began last year has been so lackluster.

To be sure, the housing market is faring better in several metro areas, particularly those with decent job growth such as parts of Texas and Washington, D.C. And the National Association of Realtors said it believed a housing recovery was already taking place, though chief economist Lawrence Yun said it would be "choppy at times."

But other economists say any talk of a recovery is premature until prices stabilize. In the Realtors' report, the median home price fell 2.4% to $171,700 in September from a year earlier.

The latest data don't reflect the suspensions of some foreclosure sales. Monday's home-sales report noted that foreclosures and other distressed sales accounted for more than one-third of transactions in September.

Earlier this month, Nina Kachakova was days from closing on a two-bedroom home in Sunrise, Fla. But Fannie Mae pulled the listing after Bank of America Corp., which had serviced the loan on the property, announced it was suspending foreclosures. In a letter, Fannie asked to extend the closing until next Tuesday, but added the "extension does not guarantee resolution of title defect within this time frame."

Ms. Kachakova, who had been outbid on nearly a dozen other properties since May, has already paid $1,800 for an appraisal, home inspection and loan-origination and processing fees for the $135,000 home. "Right now, it's just a waiting game, and I don't know if I should start all over," said the 24-year-old bookkeeper.

Some already-skittish home buyers are asking sellers to further lower their prices to offset the costs of delays.

Kelly Hoemann, 30, chose not to renew the lease on her apartment after going into contract on the $85,000 two-bedroom home in St. Petersburg, Fla., earlier this month. She now faces a foreclosure-related delay. "Come November 1, I don't have a place to live," said Ms. Hoemann, who asked Freddie Mac to consider lowering the sales price to $75,000, to give her an incentive to ride out the delay.

At the mortgage bankers' annual convention in Atlanta Monday, Fannie Mae Chief Executive Michael Williams emphasized the need to fix problems quickly "so it doesn't create an overhang of [bank-owned] property."

The recovery could be interrupted most severely in Florida, New Jersey and other "judicial" states where banks must complete foreclosures through court.

"Long after other parts of the country have burned off their excess foreclosure inventory, we will still be digesting ours, that's for sure," said Jeffrey Otteau, president of Otteau Valuation Group, an East Brunswick, N.J., appraisal firm.

At the current sales pace, it would take nearly 10.7 months to sell the nation's inventory of 4.04 million homes, according to the Monday report by the NAR. Six to eight months of inventory is considered healthy.

The housing market isn't likely to improve until job and income growth picks up. Other intractable problems include high rates of homeowners who owe more than their homes are worth, and the "shadow" inventory of loans, estimated at around four million, that are in some stage of foreclosure, or that have missed three or more mortgage payments.

Justin Lahart contributed to this article.

The 'Aquaminium' lifestyle; Latest must-have: A parking garage for your yacht
By Amy Ma

Copyright 2010 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

Would James Bond Live in an "aquaminium?"

Property developer Gulu Lalvani, says he created the term to describe an apartment complex on the water with one notable added feature —an indoor parking garage for a boat.

At the Royal Phuket Marina, where Mr. Lalvani is chief executive officer, these garages are available in just two penthouse units; the other apartments have outdoor docking stations. But according to Mr. Lalvani, who founded with his brother the U.K. consumer-electronics company Binatone, the latest must-have in luxury living is a dock just steps from your front door.

The idea came from James Bond, says Mr. Lalvani. In the movies, the British secret agent often needs to make a quick getaway by boat, and of course, a fast boat is always close at hand. (The nearby island of Khoah Phing Kan is nicknamed "James Bond Island" because it appeared in "The Man with the Golden Gun" in 1974.)

The Aquiminium complex was completed in 2009. Of the 15 apartments, two penthouses, and five villas in the development, 14 units are occupied. The remaining units are priced up to $10.5 million. A second complex is in the works.

The list of residents is a closely guarded secret. However, the marina did reveal that 10 units are apparently owned by chief executives from Hong Kong and Singapore in various industries. Among the well-to-do, according to the marina, are two Taiwanese tycoons—one in the semiconductors industry and the other in children's garments— and an Englishman who made his fortune replicating antique-furniture designs in Asia. Also joining the neighborhood is a long-term Phuket resident and famed restaurateur, the marina said. Though Mr. Lalvani is looking for buyers from mainland China, thus far, there have been none.

The marina's developers say that hobnobbing with such power folk will no doubt be a bonus to other tempted buyers. One British businessman who now lives there said, the Aquaminium community will be a "place where you can meet up with like-minded people."

Toronto-born Omar Khadr, 15 years old when captured after a 2002 firefight in Afghanistan, pleaded guilty to throwing a grenade that mortally wounded a U.S. soldier, among other charges. In exchange, the U.S. agreed to support his repatriation to Canada after he serves one additional year at Guantanamo. The now 24-year-old defendant also admitted to planting improvised explosive devices and receiving weapons training from the terrorist network.Khadr would be elgible for transfer back to his native Canada after serving the first year of his sentence as part of the agreement, said Army Col. Patrick Parrish, the military judge.[http://online.wsj.com/article/SB40001424052702304388304575574060876997810.html]

In Mexico, armed men burst into a drug rehab center in the border city of Tijuana late Sunday and police said at least 10 people were killed. A client at the center and local media reports put the number of deaths at 13. It was the second massacre of the weekend in Mexico: 14 people were killed Friday night when gunmen stormed a birthday party in another border city, Ciudad Juarez.

In Afghanistan, President Hamid Karzai said that Iran has been supplying millions of dollars in bags of cash to his office for years, but that the U.S. government was apprised of this since he personally informed then-President George W. Bush in 2002. Karzai said that his chief of staff and former Afghan ambassador to Tehran, Umar Daudzai, regularly collects the cash from Iranian officials on his orders. He said the money is used for the presidential office expenses. The Afghan president said the bundles of cash, containing between €500,000 ($696,450) and €700,000 each, were handed over by Iranian officials once or twice a year. Karzai said the United Arab Emirates has also given the presidential office a cash donation worth about $1.5 million.[http://online.wsj.com/article/SB40001424052702304388304575574001362056346.html]

In Pakistan, a bomb planted on a motorcycle exploded at the gate of a famous Sufi shrine during morning prayers, killing at least five people, officials said. The bombing at the Farid Shakar Ganj shrine in Punjab province was the latest in a string of attacks targeting Sufi shrines in Pakistan.

The leaders of India and Japan signed a broad agreement aimed at increasing trade and agreed to speed up talks toward a civilian nuclear energy deal—despite sensitivity in Japan over India's past atomic test blasts. The deal, which needs to be ratified by Japan's parliament to come into force, slashes tariffs on goods from auto parts to bonsai plants and introduces measures to promote investment and deal with intellectual property rights.

Indonesia warned its most volatile volcano could erupt at any time, prompting calls for the evacuation of thousands of villagers living on the mountain's slopes. Mount Merapi has seen increased volcanic activity over the past week and officials have raised the alert level for the 9,737-foot (2,968-meter) -high mountain to the most urgent level, said a government volcanologist. The mountain last erupted in 2006, when it sent an avalanche of blistering gases and rock fragments racing down the mountain that killed two people. A similar eruption in 1994 killed 60 people, while 1,300 people died in an eruption in 1930. Also in Indonesia, a strong earthquake hit off the western coast of Sumatra island, but no damage or casualties were immediately reported. The 7.5-magnitude temblor struck at a depth of 20 miles (30 kilometers) on a small island off Sumatra, the U.S. Geological Survey said.

In Taiwan, rescuers rappelled down a cliff trying to trace 21 Chinese tourists whose bus is believed to have tumbled off a narrow coastal road as a fierce typhoon lashed east Asia last week. Rescue officials said they have found one body and assorted body parts in the area, but no definitive identifications had yet been made.

No crew members were injured and the ship was on its way to South Africa as planned.

Real Estate
Housing Gloom Deepens; Home Sales Rise, but Economists Don't See Prices Bottoming Till Late '11 or '12
By Nick Timiraos
1248 words
26 October 2010
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2010 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

Home sales picked up in September, but the long-term picture for housing is growing grimmer, say analysts and economists who are pushing back forecasts for a housing recovery.

Earlier this year, the housing market appeared poised for a turnaround, three years after it peaked. Federal tax credits for buyers spurred a flurry of activity, and the economy was adding jobs. That led some economists to forecast housing would hit bottom and begin to recover this year.

Now, some economists don't see a recovery until late next year or early 2012. "In most markets, the tide seems to be going back out," said Stan Humphries, chief economist at Zillow.com, a real-estate site. "The momentum is easing."

Adding to the mounting worries is the foreclosure crisis. Some banks suspended sales of foreclosed homes late last month to address questions about the integrity of the foreclosure process. If a substantial part of the market freezes for some weeks, that could further crimp sales.

On Monday, the National Association of Realtors said sales of previously owned homes ran at a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 4.53 million in September. While that was up 10% from August, the overall level of sales remains weak, and economists believe sales going forward could fall again.

Indeed, half of the 109 economists and housing analysts polled this month by MacroMarkets LLC, a housing-futures firm, expect home prices to bottom next year, but half don't expect a rebound until 2012.

The growing pessimism is attributed partly to rising inventory in many markets, a trend that doesn't bode well for prices. The Wall Street Journal's latest quarterly survey of housing-market conditions in 28 major metropolitan areas found inventories of unsold homes were up in 19 markets at the end of the third quarter, compared with a year ago, with especially large increases in San Diego, Los Angeles and Sacramento.

"We'll see some additional price declines," said David Berson, chief economist at PMI Group Inc., a mortgage-insurance company in Walnut Creek, Calif. "The gains we've seen can't be sustained given the current supply situation."

The housing market typically plays a big role as the economy exits from recession, with a rebound in new home building sparking demand for construction workers and building supplies. "We're clearly not getting that this time," said MF Global economist James O'Sullivan. It's a major reason, he said, that the recovery that began last year has been so lackluster.

To be sure, the housing market is faring better in several metro areas, particularly those with decent job growth such as parts of Texas and Washington, D.C. And the National Association of Realtors said it believes a housing recovery is already taking place, though chief economist Lawrence Yun said it would be "choppy at times."

But other economists say any talk of a recovery is premature until prices stabilize. In the Realtors' report, the median home price fell 2.4% to $171,700 in September from a year earlier.

The latest data don't reflect the suspensions of some foreclosure sales. Monday's home-sales report noted that foreclosures and other distressed sales accounted for more than one-third of transactions in September.

"This mess threatens to dampen activity in the one area where we see the market strengthening," said Michael Feder, chief executive of real-estate data firm Radar Logic Inc., referring to foreclosure sales. "There's no way this helps. This is bad for the housing market, full stop."

Earlier this month, Nina Kachakova was days from closing on a two-bedroom home in Sunrise, Fla. But Fannie Mae pulled the listing after Bank of America Corp., which had serviced the loan on the property, announced it was suspending foreclosures. In a letter, Fannie asked to extend the closing until next Tuesday, but added the "extension does not guarantee resolution of title defect within this time frame."

Ms. Kachakova, who had been outbid on nearly a dozen other properties since May, has already paid $1,800 for an appraisal, home inspection and loan-origination and processing fees for the $135,000 home. "Right now, it's just a waiting game, and I don't know if I should start all over," said the 24-year-old bookkeeper.

Some already-skittish home buyers are asking sellers to further lower their prices to offset the costs of delays.

Kelly Hoemann, 30, chose not to renew the lease on her apartment after going into contract on the $85,000 two-bedroom home in St. Petersburg, Fla., earlier this month. She now faces a foreclosure-related delay. "Come November 1, I don't have a place to live," said Ms. Hoemann, who asked Freddie Mac to consider lowering the sales price to $75,000, to give her an incentive to ride out the delay.

At the mortgage bankers' annual convention in Atlanta Monday, Fannie Mae Chief Executive Michael Williams emphasized the need to fix problems quickly "so it doesn't create an overhang of [bank-owned] property."

The recovery could be interrupted most severely in Florida, New Jersey and other "judicial" states where banks must complete foreclosures through court. Listings of bank-owned homes in Florida's Miami-Dade, Broward and Palm Beach counties last week were down by 19% from two weeks earlier, according to Condo Vultures, a real-estate research firm.

"Long after other parts of the country have burned off their excess foreclosure inventory, we will still be digesting ours, that's for sure," said Jeffrey Otteau, president of Otteau Valuation Group, an East Brunswick, N.J., appraisal firm.

At the current sales pace, it would take nearly 10.7 months to sell the nation's inventory of 4.04 million homes, according to the Monday report by the NAR. Six to eight months of inventory is considered healthy.

The housing market isn't likely to improve until job and income growth picks up. Other intractable problems include high rates of homeowners who owe more than their homes are worth, and the "shadow" inventory of loans, estimated at around four million, that are in some stage of foreclosure, or that have missed three or more mortgage payments.

"Joe Consumer doesn't know the term 'shadow inventory,' but they're hearing more and more that the banks are going to be dumping more homes for sale next year, so they're thinking, 'Why should I buy this year?" said John Burns, an Irvine, Calif., housing consultant.

On Tuesday, the S&P/Case-Shiller index will report home prices for the three-month period ended in August, offering the first indication of how sellers have responded to the drop-off in sales earlier this summer. Economists at Goldman Sachs Group Inc. forecast that prices in August and September declined by an estimated 0.4% and 0.5%, respectively. Their estimates are based on reported declines in sellers' asking prices over that time period.

Housing markets could be in far worse shape without mortgage rates that are at their lowest rates in nearly 60 years. The average 30-year fixed-rate mortgage stood at 4.21% last week with 0.8% in fees, according to a survey by Freddie Mac.

Justin Lahart contributed to this article.

Economy
Home Resales Jump
By Sarah N. Lynch And Jeff Bater
619 words
25 October 2010
16:58
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2010 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

In a sign of improvement in housing market conditions, existing home sales rose by more than expected in September.

Sales of used homes increased by 10.0% to an annual rate of 4.53 million, the National Association of Realtors said Monday. Economists surveyed by Dow Jones Newswires had expected existing home sales to rise by 5.3% to an annual rate of 4.35 million.

The increase in home sales was the second in a row. In August, resales rose 7.3% to a rate of 4.12 million, revised down from an originally reported 7.6% increase to a 4.13 million rate.

Still, even with this latest increase, existing home sales are well below where they were a year ago. Year-over-year, existing home sales were down 19.1% from an annual rate of a 5.60 million-unit pace in September 2009. Lawrence Yun, the chief economist at NAR, said a 5 million unit pace is the mark he's looking for as a sign that the housing market can once again "stand on its feet."

This is the third month of new data since a government tax credit for home buyers has expired.

"It is still much lower than what I would consider to be fully healthy, but at least a recovery is taking place," he said. "It is a natural healing process without that stimulus medicine."

The housing market has had a hard time recovering from the bursting of the industry's bubble. The federal government created a subsidy for first-time home buyers to help the sector, but that tax credit expired last spring, causing home sales to plunge.

And as the housing industry has been struggling to recover, another crisis has emerged. The state and federal government has launched an investigation into foreclosure practices amid growing concerns about mortgage servicers' use of "robo-signers" to approve large numbers of foreclosure documents without reviewing their contents.

Several major banks including Bank of America, Ally Financial Inc.'s GMAC Mortgage and J.P. Morgan had temporarily halted foreclosures, although foreclosures in some states have since resumed.

Bank of America initially insisted there were no problems with the foreclosures, but just acknowledged for the first time some mistakes were made including misspelled names, an incorrect address and a missing signature.

Some have worried the recent concerns over foreclosures will have a chilling effect on an already-struggling housing market.

Meanwhile, low mortgage rates haven't been enough to entice Would-be home buyers thanks to high unemployment and other signs of a still-soft economy.

Prices have fallen since the housing bubble popped. While lower prices are tempting, there is a downside. Some would-be buyers watching prices dropping will wait for a better deal. That hurts sales in the near-term. Another problem is some owners, waiting for prices to rise, will keep their property off the market, adding to inventory. High inventories add more pressure to already depressed home prices.

The data Monday said the median price for an existing home was $171,700 in September. That's down 2.4% from $175,900 in September 2009.

Inventories of used homes decreased by 1.9% at the end of September to 4.04 million available for sale. That represented a 10.7-month supply at the current sales pace, compared with a 12.0-month supply in August.

Regionally in September, existing home sales increased 5.0% in the West compared to the prior month, 10.1% in the Northeast, 10.6% in the South, and 14.5% in the Midwest.

Dan Fitzpatrick and Jared Favole contributed to this article.

World News: U.S. home construction grows
By Justin Lahart and Jeffrey Sparshott
266 words
20 October 2010
The Wall Street Journal Asia
AWSJ
9
English
(c) 2010 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. To see the edition in which this article appeared, click here http://awsj.com.hk/factiva-ns[http://awsj.com.hk/factiva-ns]

U.S. home construction rose last month, suggesting that the lull in the housing market that followed the expiration of home-buyer tax breaks in the spring has ended.

September construction starts on single-family homes rose 4.4% from August, an annual rate of 468,000, the Commerce Department said Tuesday. Overall housing starts, which include groundbreakings on apartment and other multifamily buildings, increased 0.3% to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 610,000.

Even though the level of monthly housing starts is up from an average of 530,000 in the first quarter of 2009, by the standards of the past 50 years it is still low, noted New York Federal Reserve Bank President William Dudley in remarks to reporters Tuesday.

"One reason why so little housing is being built is that many existing homes stand vacant," he said. "We estimate that there are roughly three million vacant housing units more than usual. And more vacancies are added daily as the foreclosure process moves homes from families to mortgage lenders."

Bank of America Corp., after imposing a moratorium on foreclosures earlier this month on documentation concerns, reopened more than 100,000 foreclosure actions Monday.

The combination of weak demand and the large supply of vacant homes for sale will leave housing starts subdued for several years, said Paul Dales, economist at Capital Economics. "We doubt that housing starts will reach one million before 2015," he said.

WASHINGTON, D.C.

New Home Construction

Rose 4.4% in September

U.S. home construction rose last month, suggesting that the lull in the housing market that followed the expiration of home-buyer tax breaks in the spring has ended.

September construction starts on single-family homes rose 4.4% to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 452,000, the Commerce Department said Tuesday.

Overall housing starts, which include groundbreakings on apartment and other multifamily buildings, increased 0.3% from August to an annual rate of 610,000.

Even though the level of monthly housing starts is up from an average of 530,000 in the first quarter of 2009, by the standards of the past 50 years, it is still low, noted New York Federal Reserve Bank President William Dudley in remarks to reporters Tuesday.

"One reason why so little housing is being built is that many existing homes stand vacant," he said. "We estimate that there are roughly three million vacant housing units more than usual."

-- Justin Lahart and Jeffrey Sparshott

---

PENTAGON

Military Recruiters Told

To Accept Gay Applicants

The Pentagon said it has ordered recruiters to accept applications from gays and lesbians.

The move came as a California federal judge rejected a U.S. government request to delay her week-old order barring the military from enforcing the "don't ask, don't tell" law against gays. U.S. District Judge Virginia Phillips in Riverside, Calif., said the law "harms military readiness and unit cohesion, and irreparably injures service members by violating their fundamental rights," citing evidence from a trial in July.

The judge issued the injunction Oct. 12, and her Tuesday decision was expected. The Obama administration says it wants to reverse the policy that excludes openly gay people from serving in the military but believes the change should be made by Congress, not the courts.

The Pentagon says it is complying with the injunction and has stopped enforcing the law, but pointed out that Judge Phillips's injunction could be reversed.

-- Evan Perez and Julian E. Barnes

TEXAS

Jordanian Gets 24 Years

In Dallas Bomb Plot

A Jordanian man caught in an FBI sting trying to blow up a Dallas skyscraper was sentenced Tuesday to 24 years in prison after telling the court he was ashamed of his actions and renouncing al Qaeda.

Hosam Smadi, 20 years old, faced up to life in prison but received a reduced sentence after pleading guilty in May to attempted use of a weapon of mass destruction.

"I'm so ashamed for what I did. I'm very sorry for my actions," Mr. Smadi told U.S. District Judge Barbara Lynn just before he was sentenced. "I could not live with myself if I had hurt anybody."

Mr. Smadi acknowledged leaving what he thought was a truck bomb in a garage beneath the 60-story Fountain Place building in September 2009.

-- Associated Press

WASHINGTON, D.C.

American Indian Farmers

Are Offered $680 Million

The Obama administration agreed Tuesday to pay up to $680 million to American Indian farmers to settle an 11-year-old class-action lawsuit alleging discrimination by the U.S. Department of Agriculture.

Agriculture Secretary Tom Vilsack, who took office promising to address longstanding complaints by minority farmers against the department, said eligible farmers and ranchers can receive up to $250,000 each for showing that USDA discrimination caused them economic losses. However, most farmers will probably opt for a uniform $50,000 payment, which involves less red tape.

In the class-action suit, American Indian farmers allege that USDA bureaucrats denied them the low-interest rate loans given to white farmers between 1981 and 2007.

-- Scott Kilman

CALIFORNIA

Food Trucks to Post Grades

The Los Angeles County supervisors voted unanimously Tuesday to require the posting of health-board ratings on thousands of food trucks. The ordinance will take effect in 30 days. County health inspectors will have the authority to conduct surprise visits to the trucks twice a year and give them A, B or C ratings, the same as they do with restaurants.

-- Associated Press

Property: Region Trails City's Rebound, N.Y. Fed Says
By Shelly Banjo
350 words
20 October 2010
The Wall Street Journal
J
A26
English
(Copyright (c) 2010, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

Although New York is recovering from the economic downturn, the rest of the region isn't following suit, New York Federal Reserve President William Dudley said Tuesday in an update on regional economic conditions.

"In New Jersey, although activity is no longer declining, there has yet to be a sustained recovery," Mr. Dudley said. "Its economy remains essentially flat."

The region's housing cycle has been less severe than in other parts of the country, as home prices stabilize and sales rise, he said.

Still, the New York Fed says it continues to monitor factors that could undercut the home prices in New York state, the 12 northern counties of New Jersey and Fairfield County, Conn., which it oversees.

Those factors include little to no job growth in high-paying sectors like the securities markets; uncertainty over the financial industry and the size of this year's bonuses; the tens of thousands of new housing units coming down the pipeline; and the widening gap between home prices and rents, which is making renting more attractive than buying for consumers, according to a presentation by Jason Bram, a senior economist at the New York Fed.

Private-sector employment in New York state and New Jersey is fairly flat over its year-ago level and government hiring remains weak, the Fed's presentation illustrated.

Unemployment rates hover at exceptionally high levels: August's seasonally adjusted rates topped 9.8% in New Jersey's Bergen and Hudson counties and 9.4% in New York City, compared with the national rate of 9.6%.

The Fed said the region was spared the worst effects of the mortgage boom and bust, with fewer delinquencies and foreclosures than the rest of the country, but pockets of housing distress persist in the Bronx, Queens and Long Island.

Despite the pullback, Manhattan's average home prices are still 3 1/2 times as high as in 1995, a period over which rents roughly doubled, the Fed said.

English
(Copyright (c) 2010, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

U.S. home construction rose last month, suggesting that the lull in the housing market that followed the expiration of home-buyer tax breaks in the spring has ended.

September construction starts on single-family homes rose 4.4% to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 452,000, the Commerce Department said Tuesday. Overall housing starts, which include groundbreakings on apartment and other multifamily buildings, increased 0.3% from August to an annual rate of 610,000.

Even though the level of monthly housing starts is up from an average of 530,000 in the first quarter of 2009, by the standards of the past 50 years, it is still low, noted New York Federal Reserve Bank President William Dudley in remarks to reporters Tuesday.

"One reason why so little housing is being built is that many existing homes stand vacant," he said. "We estimate that there are roughly three million vacant housing units more than usual. And more vacancies are added daily as the foreclosure process moves homes from families to mortgage lenders."

Bank of America Corp., after imposing a moratorium on foreclosures earlier this month amid documentation concerns, reopened more than 100,000 foreclosure actions Monday.

The combination of weak demand and the large supply of vacant homes for sale will leave housing starts subdued for several years, said Paul Dales, economist at Capital Economics. "We doubt that housing starts will reach one million before 2015," he said.

Regionally, housing starts in September increased 4.8% in the South and 2.9% in the Northeast. They fell 8.2% in the Midwest, and 3.6% in the West.

Building permits for single-homes, seen as an indicator of future construction, edged up to 405,000 in September from 403,000 in August. But weighed down by a drop in permits for multifamily homes, overall permits fell by 5.6% to 571,000.

New York Real Estate
Region Trails City's Rebound, N.Y. Fed Says
By Shelly Banjo
353 words
20 October 2010
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2010 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

Although New York is recovering from the economic downturn, the rest of the region isn't following suit, New York Federal Reserve President William Dudley said Tuesday in an update on regional economic conditions.

"In New Jersey, although activity is no longer declining, there has yet to be a sustained recovery," Mr. Dudley said. "Its economy remains essentially flat."

The region's housing cycle has been less severe than in other parts of the country, as home prices stabilize and sales rise, he said.

Still, the New York Fed says it continues to monitor factors that could undercut the home prices in New York state, the 12 northern counties of New Jersey and Fairfield County, Conn., which it oversees.

Those factors include little to no job growth in high-paying sectors like the securities markets; uncertainty over the financial industry and the size of this year's bonuses; the tens of thousands of new housing units coming down the pipeline; and the widening gap between home prices and rents, which is making renting more attractive than buying for consumers, according to a presentation by Jason Bram, a senior economist at the New York Fed.

Private-sector employment in New York state and New Jersey is fairly flat over its year-ago level and government hiring remains weak, the Fed's presentation illustrated.

Unemployment rates hover at exceptionally high levels: August's seasonally adjusted rates topped 9.8% in New Jersey's Bergen and Hudson counties and 9.4% in New York City, compared with the national rate of 9.6%.

The Fed said the region was spared the worst effects of the mortgage boom and bust, with fewer delinquencies and foreclosures than the rest of the country, but pockets of housing distress persist in the Bronx, Queens and Long Island.

Despite the pullback, Manhattan's average home prices are still 3½ times as high as in 1995, a period over which rents roughly doubled, the Fed said.

Commercial Property: Central Bronx Set for Revitalization
By Eliot Brown
442 words
18 October 2010
The Wall Street Journal
J
A24
English
(Copyright (c) 2010, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

A rezoning of 75 blocks in the central Bronx breezed through the city's land-use-approval process. But the next step promises to be more difficult: encouraging new construction in a long-overlooked part of the borough at a time of economic uncertainty.

The City Council last week gave its final approval to the rezoning proposal, which revamps development restrictions that have been in place since 1961 -- a time when an elevated train still rumbled above Third Avenue. Together with another plan now under review to rezone a nearly two-mile stretch of Webster Avenue, city planners hope to revitalize and create new housing in a swath of the Bronx north and west of the New York Botanical Gardens and the Bronx Zoo.

The plan adopted last week permits residential and commercial uses on the stretch of Third Avenue from the Cross-Bronx Expressway to Fordham Road, replacing regulations that largely called for automotive shops and light-industrial facilities there. The city hopes that new regulations allowing larger buildings to be built will create a more bustling business district along East Tremont Avenue, one of the only shopping areas in the central Bronx.

The tracks of the Third Avenue "El," which guided many of the area's longtime zoning regulations, were demolished in 1973. The new plan will "reactivate Third Avenue and reinforce Tremont Avenue as the area's downtown," City Planning Commissioner Amanda Burden said when proposing the rezoning in May.

But community leaders now have to wait to see whether developers choose to take advantage of the rezoning despite the uncertain economy. "There may be people out there who were just waiting for this to happen," says Ivene Galarza, district manager for the area's community board. "Things will start materializing from here on."

One optimistic developer is Radame Perez of Mastermind Management Ltd., a Bronx-based company that owns apartments and commercial space across the New York area. Mr. Perez has a plan to build a high-rise apartment building, the Tremont Renaissance, on East Tremont Avenue with as much as 100,000 square feet of commercial space. He says the new zoning will allow him to increase the size of the project from about 120 residential units to at least 160, and he predicts he'll be ready to start construction as early as next year.

"We have to configure apartment sizes and really start to get to work now," Mr. Perez says. "We'll be able to present our plan now to the banks and to the city."
