Asia Markets
Asian Stocks Mixed
By V. Phani Kumar in Hong Kong and Colin Ng and Leslie Shaffer in Singapore
1230 words
30 September 2010
05:02
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2010 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

HONG KONG—Major Asian markets ended mostly down Thursday on reemerging economic worries about Europe and after a decline on Wall Street, with Japanese shares tumbling as the yen rallied against the U.S. dollar. Nintendo Co. plunged after slashing its profit forecasts.

Chinese shares rebounded to end sharply higher as property developers soared a day after Beijing introduced tightening measures that weren't as harsh as some feared. The measures were "somewhat weaker than what the market had expected," said Wu Jianxiong from Central China Securities. "The real-estate tax was the biggest worry, but there were no details about its implementation."

Japan's Nikkei Stock Average fell 2%, extending losses in afternoon trade, with Australia's S&P/ASX 200 falling 1.3%, Hong Kong's Hang Seng Index slipping 0.1% and China's Shanghai Composite gaining 1.7%. Hong Kong is closed Friday and Chinese markets are closed from Friday through next Thursday for the National Day holiday.

Chinese shares advanced though Beijing took more steps Wednesday to cool stubbornly high property prices, ordering banks to halt lending for third and subsequent home purchases and raising the minimum down payment for all first-time home buyers. The statement from the State Council, China's cabinet, also said the government aims to speed up a trial reform of a "real estate tax" now being carried out in some cities.

"They have been using this [tax] as one of the means to dampen the property market, but they don't want to kill the market. So it's probably been held in reserve. It's a reminder for people not to get too enthusiastic," said Howard Gorges, vice chairman at South China Brokerages. "I think that the government likes to make people feel good about the stock market before they go on a holiday. So, I suspect there might be a little window-dressing in Shanghai," he added.

On the mainland market, China Vanke surged 7.6% and Poly Real Estate spiked 8.9%.

However, the Hong Kong market took a different view of the measures, with China developers listed there dropping sharply. Credit Suisse noted in a report that they have rallied more than their mainland peers since mid-May. Shimao Property Holdings fell 3% and China Resources Land declined 4.4%.

Hong Kong-listed shares of Industrial & Commercial Bank of China dropped 3.2%, with Goldman Sachs selling part of its stake in the mainland bank. A person familiar with the matter told Dow Jones Newswires Goldman raised $2.25 billion from the sale of 3.04 billion shares.

Elsewhere in the region, South Korea's Kospi added 0.3%, Taiwan's Taiex ended little changed. In afternoon trading, India's Sensex slipped 0.2% and Indonesian shares were up 0.1%.

Dow Jones Industrial Average futures were down 16 points in screen trade.

Nintendo Co. led the fall in Tokyo, slumping 10.2% after it said on Wednesday its highly anticipated 3DS hand-held videogame machine will miss the holiday-shopping season and slashed its profit forecasts for the year by more than half.

Tokyo Electric Power shed 3.3% after it said it will raise up to $6.6 billion to finance massive energy-related capital outlays at home and abroad, in what would be the largest equity financing among Japanese nonfinancial companies this year.

Citizen Holdings gained 0.2% on a Nikkei report that the watch maker's April-September group operating profit was expected to quadruple from a year earlier to around seven billion yen ($84.3 million).

"Investors are still worried about the yen's strength, so they don't want to chase the market higher," said Hikaru Sato, senior technical analyst at Daiwa Securities Capital Markets.

Financial shares lost ground after the cost of insuring debt for consumer lenders Acom Co. and Promise Co. rose sharply in the wake of peer Takefuji's bankruptcy-protection filing this week. Mitsubishi UFJ Financial Group, which backs Acom, dropped 3.7% and Sumitomo Mitsui Financial, which backs Promise, fell 3.9%. Promise tumbled 6.4% and Acom dropped 3.1%.

In South Korea, technology and transportation stocks helped to support the market. "Investors seem to be buying major stocks whose third-quarter earnings forecasts are strong," said Kim Jung-hoon at Korea Investment & Securities.

Hynix Semiconductor rose 1.4%, while Korean Air Lines Co. added 2.1% and Korea Kumho Petrochemical advanced 3.1%.

In Mumbai, Satyam Computer Services dropped 7.8% after reporting a fiscal year loss of 1.25 billion rupees ($27.8 million), compared with expectations for a net profit.

The Sydney stock market was weighed by rising concern over sovereign-debt issues in Europe and the release of data that showed a drop in domestic hom-building approvals for August and weaker property prices in the nation's cities. Banks paced the fall, with Australia & New Zealand Banking Group shedding 1.9% and Westpac Banking Corp. sliding 2%.

"Concern over European banks and Ireland could be weighing on the market," said Peter Copeland, senior institutional trader at BBY in Sydney. Strikes have ground much of Spain to a halt as workers in Europe crank up protests against austerity measures.

Ratings agency Moody's Thursday downgraded Spain's sovereign rating to Aa1 from Aaa, with a stable outlook, citing a "considerable deterioration" in government finances and worsening debt affordability. Furthermore, the cost of supporting the Irish banking system will cause the government's budget deficit to rise to 32% of its gross domestic product this year, Finance Minister Brian Lenihen said.

Elsewhere, New Zealand's NZX 50 fell 1.5% and Philippine shares ended 0.3% lower. By late afternoon, Singapore's Straits Times Index fell 0.3% and Thailand's SET Index rose 0.6%.

In the foreign-exchange markets, the yen resumed its climb as investors awaited U.S. indicators such as weekly jobless claims and the Chicago purchasing-managers' index. Masafumi Yamamoto, chief foreign-exchange strategist in Japan for Barclays Bank, said worse-than-expected U.S. data would boost speculation that the Federal Reserve may soon take additional quantitative easing measures, putting pressure on the U.S. dollar.

The dollar was at 83.25 yen from 83.70 yen in late New York Wednesday, while the euro was at 113.34 yen from 114.07 yen. Against the dollar, the euro was at $1.3612 from $1.3630.

Lead December Japanese government bond futures were up 0.02 at 143.42 points, tracking Wednesday's weakness in U.S. Treasurys, while the yield on the 10-year Japanese government bond rose 1.5 basis points to 0.935%. JGB prices were supported by an unexpected fall in Japan's industrial production in August, as it strengthened the view that the Bank of Japan would soon loosen monetary policy further to support the economy. Industrial output slipped 0.3% on month in August, compared to a 1.1% increase forecast.

Spot gold was at $1,311.40 per troy ounce, up $1.50 from its New York close Wednesday. Nymex November crude-oil futures were up seven cents at $77.93 per barrel.

Commercial Real Estate
High Hopes as Builders Bet on Skyscrapers; With Financing Scarce, Developers Use Their Own Cash to Wager on a Coming Resurgence in Office-Space Demand
By Anton Troianovski
985 words
29 September 2010
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2010 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

The office-vacancy rate in Rosslyn, Va., is one of the lowest in the Washington region, which may well be the healthiest office market in the country.

Even so, developer Anthony Westreich hasn't been able to secure a construction loan for a 35-story, $300 million office project on a prime piece of land in Rosslyn. But he says the partnership behind the project will break ground next month anyway and spend $30 million in cash to start construction—a risky bet on the local market and an illustration of just how hard it is to build a skyscraper these days.

"It's a bit of a cart-and-horse situation," says Mr. Westreich, chief executive of closely held New York and Washington landlord Monday Properties. "You have tenants saying, 'I don't want to commit to a lease in your building until I know the financing is there,' and lenders saying, 'I don't want to commit to financing your building until I know the tenants are there.' "

The dearth of financing is forcing developers to get creative about ways to proceed with projects that have been languishing on the drawing board for years. During the boom, developers often were able to obtain financing for office-building or retail projects before signing tenants.

But ever since the recession hit, the financing spigot has run dry. Steep losses on top-of-the-market deals and a drop in demand for space have put have builders on the sidelines, while many lenders are swamped with boom-time loans gone bad.

Outstanding construction loans backing office buildings, shopping malls and other kinds of commercial real estate fell to a total of $221 billion in the second quarter of 2010, down from a peak of $298 billion in early 2009, according to data firm Foresight Analytics. The portion of those loans 30 days or more past due is up to 15.6%, nearly four times the rate two years ago. The nationwide vacancy rate for office buildings reached 17.4% in the second quarter, the highest level since 1993, according to research firm Reis Inc.

"I just don't see people taking risk," says Stephen Blank, who specializes in real-estate finance for the nonprofit Urban Land Institute. "Sure, people have short memories—but not that short."

The lack of financing and demand are among the reasons the construction industry is still in the doldrums. The Associated General Contractors of America says 245 out of 337 metropolitan areas lost construction jobs in the 12-month period that ended in August, with the unemployment rate in the construction industry remaining at nearly twice the national average. Demand for design services is still declining, according to the American Institute of Architects' billings index—a sign that an uptick in new construction isn't expected in the next nine to 12 months.

But some developers think all that gloom makes for opportunity, arguing that a lack of new construction means demand for state-of-the-art buildings could outstrip supply within a few years. In New York City, developer Douglas Durst is making the second bet of his career on constructing a major office building as much of the real-estate industry is still reeling from a downturn. He recently won a competition to invest about $100 million in the 1,776-foot-tall skyscraper being built by the Port Authority of New York and New Jersey on the site of the Sept. 11, 2001, terrorist attacks.

In the mid-1990s, Mr. Durst broke ground on a skyscraper in Times Square without having found any tenants and while many competitors were still struggling with the aftermath of the recession of the early 1990s. He secured an anchor tenant for the building, publisher Condé Nast, before it was completed in 1999.

"The theory in 1994 and now is that there are very few other buildings being built, and tenants always prefer a new building," Mr. Durst said in an interview. "The risk is that the downturn lasts longer and you come online with an empty building."

Other developers of new projects have been looking beyond banks for financing. Extell Development Co. has started construction on a 1,000-foot-tall residential tower in Manhattan, thanks in part to financing from partners in the Middle East. Extell says it is still working to secure a bank construction loan.

Developers in Dallas were able to start a 42-story condominium project this summer after granting 100% ownership to the local pension fund financing the project.

Outside Washington, Monday Properties' Mr. West reich also hopes to complete his building at a time when little new office space is on the market. Monday Properties and its majority partner, the estate of Lehman Brothers Holdings Inc., will spend $30 million in cash on building infrastructure and a four-story, below-grade parking garage over the next 12 to 14 months. Monday Properties says starting construction will help persuade tenants and lenders to sign on. That will allow the building to be completed as early as in 2013.

The project at 1812 North Moore St. in Rosslyn had been on hold for two years as the economy sputtered. Now, Mr. Westreich says he believes the timing is right, with his nine-building Rosslyn office portfolio 96% occupied and little construction being finished from 2011 to 2013.

"We think we're coming to the market at the exact right time," Mr. Westreich says. Monday Properties' tenants in Rosslyn include defense contractors Northrop Grumman Corp. and Raytheon Co., the U.S. State Department, and Allbritton Communications Co., which owns several television stations and Politico, the Beltway news outlet.

Write to Anton Troianovski at anton.troianovski@wsj.com[mailto:anton.troianovski@wsj.com]

Homes
Real Estate Stings a Backer; CEO Faces $230 Million Hit Tied to Lehman's Collapse
By Robbie Whelan
1015 words
29 September 2010
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2010 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

The real-estate bust has taken down hundreds of banks and property companies. In most cases, the executives of those companies haven't had to take personal financial hits. But Bruce Elieff could become a high-profile exception.

Mr. Elieff is chief executive of SunCal Cos., a large, closely held California land developer and home builder. The company ran into trouble in 2008 when its biggest lender, Lehman Brothers Holdings Inc., filed for bankruptcy, forcing SunCal to suspend work on 20 large residential property developments.

Now, if SunCal can't complete the projects soon or sell them to a new developer who can, Mr. Elieff could be personally on the hook for as much as $230 million. Lehman has said it intends to hold him to his guarantees.

Whether and how much Mr. Elieff must pay will be determined by the outcome of the Lehman Brothers bankruptcy liquidation. Lehman says Mr. Elieff is liable, but he said in an interview he is confident Lehman would ultimately be held responsible for paying the obligations. Mr. Elieff's lawyer Louis "Skip" Miller, a partner at Miller Barondess LLP, said his client "never anticipated being obligated to pay—he's now looking at having to defend and deal with bond claims that otherwise would have been paid by Lehman." Last year, Mr. Elieff filed suit against Lehman in U.S. Bankruptcy Court in Santa Ana, Calif., to restructure the debt and cancel the bond obligations.

Mr. Elieff's predicament is unusual these days: Developers were far more likely to risk and lose their personal fortunes during the real-estate collapse of the early 1990s, and many learned their lessons during the last cycle. Donald Trump, for example, who was pushed to the brink in the early 1990s, avoided investing any of his own money or signing for any of the debt on most of the projects that have carried his name in the past 15 years. When some, like the Trump SoHo, ran into financial problems, he wasn't exposed to any personal risk.

One of the other few exceptions to this rule in the current downturn was New York developer Harry Macklowe. He lost to creditors or was forced to sell much of his empire, including Manhattan's General Motors building, to resolve a debt he had personally guaranteed.

The hard-charging son of a Macedonian immigrant, Mr. Elieff and his brother Stephan built their father's small real-estate business into the largest land developer in the western U.S., worth $4 billion. Mr. Elieff was listed as the 25th-wealthiest person in Orange County in 2007, with an estimated personal fortune of $300 million, according to the Orange County Business Journal. But the following year he was taken off the list because of the "homebuilding downturn that has lowered values of SunCal land," according to the business paper.

Before it collapsed, Lehman had extended credit of $2.2 billion to SunCal. The 20 stalled projects—which were forced to seek bankruptcy protection—represent about $1.8 billion of those loans, and a spokeswoman for Lehman said the company is "actively pursuing" the resolution of those debts.

Mr. Elieff signed personal guarantees on $230 million worth of surety bonds related to these now-bankrupt Lehman-funded projects.

In California, developers are typically required to enter into bonding agreements before a local government gives them the green light build. The builder pledges to construct roads, sewers, bridges and other public infrastructure for the new community, and these pledges are guaranteed personally by the developer—in this case, Mr. Elieff.

If Mr. Elieff isn't able to persuade the courts that Lehman is responsible for the $230 million in obligations, a judge could order him to cover them from his own personal assets.

This has happened before in similar circumstances. Earlier this summer, a California state judge signed an order of attachment on Mr. Elieff's personal property, including three houses in Orange County, one of them in a luxury golfing community in Newport Beach. The order stemmed from a dispute over a $9.2 million distressed loan from Central Pacific Bank, guaranteed by Mr. Elieff, with which SunCal had planned to help fund a housing development in Alameda, Calif.

Central Pacific couldn't be immediately reached for comment, but the Alameda debt was sold as part of a portfolio of distressed loans in July 2008 to an investment group led by San Diego investor Keith Ayres. Mr. Ayres said in an interview that personal guarantees are "extremely typical" for land loans in California, and said he and his investors intend to pursue Mr. Elieff's assets if the note isn't paid in full.

According to court filings in a California bankruptcy court, Mr. Elieff's signature and personal guarantee are attached to surety bonds on 10 residential developments, including $56.5 million of bonds related to Marblehead Coastal property, a planned 248-acre luxury housing and commercial development along the ocean in San Clemente. The city has demanded payment on $33 million of those bonds.

Other cities, including Palmdale, San Juan Capistrano and Beaumont, have demanded payment for bonds on SunCal projects that are personally guaranteed by Mr. Elieff. He hasn't yet been forced to cover these obligations personally, but he can be compelled to do so.

"It's really critical to get moving again," said David Dillon, economic-development director of Beaumont. "SunCal had no money....In this real estate market, there are a lot of personal guarantees out there, and SunCal is one of the biggest."

Mr. Elieff said in an interview that he is confident Lehman would ultimately be held responsible for paying the bond obligations, citing an agreement last year to indemnify him in event of a default. Lehman denies that such an agreement was ever signed. The matter is being discussed as part of the continuing litigation between Lehman and SunCal.

Write to Robbie Whelan at robbie.whelan@wsj.com[mailto:robbie.whelan@wsj.com]

He secured an anchor tenant for the building, publisher Conde Nast, before it was completed in 1999.

"The theory in 1994 and now is that there are very few other buildings being built, and tenants always prefer a new building," Mr. Durst said in an interview. "The risk is that the downturn lasts longer and you come online with an empty building."

Other developers of new projects have been looking beyond banks for financing. Extell Development Co. has started construction on a 1,000-foot-tall residential tower in Manhattan, thanks in part to financing from partners in the Middle East. Extell says it is still working to secure a bank construction loan.

Developers in Dallas were able to start a 42-story condominium project this summer after granting 100% ownership to the local pension fund financing the project.

Outside Washington, Monday Properties' Mr. West reich also hopes to complete his building at a time when little new office space is on the market. Monday Properties and its majority partner, the estate of Lehman Brothers Holdings Inc., will spend $30 million in cash on building infrastructure and a four-story, below-grade parking garage over the next 12 to 14 months. Monday Properties says starting construction will help persuade tenants and lenders to sign on. That will allow the building to be completed as early as in 2013.

The project at 1812 North Moore St. in Rosslyn had been on hold for two years as the economy sputtered. Now, Mr. Westreich says he believes the timing is right, with his nine-building Rosslyn office portfolio 96% occupied and little construction being finished from 2011 to 2013.

"We think we're coming to the market at the exact right time," Mr. Westreich says. Monday Properties' tenants in Rosslyn include defense contractors Northrop Grumman Corp. and Raytheon Co., the U.S. State Department, and Allbritton Communications Co., which owns several television stations and Politico, the Beltway news outlet.

New York Real Estate
Brokers Expand Firm With Mortgage Unit
By Anton Troianovski
1134 words
28 September 2010
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2010 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

In 1988, just a year before a buzz saw hit New York's commercial real-estate market, two 20-something brokers decided it was a good time to start their own firm specializing in selling small- and midsize property.

That firm managed to survive the onslaught and grew to a large player in the market. Now, 22 years later, Paul Massey and Robert Knakal are planning to roll the dice again. Massey Knakal Realty Services is getting into the business of arranging mortgages for commercial properties.

But the timing is different for this venture. While Massey Knakal rode the last downdraft to the bottom, the new unit's debut comes as commercial sales in the New York market are showing signs of emerging from the recession upheaval. Deal volume is increasing,say, as property values seem to be stabilizing, financing options are growing and more lenders try to sell off foreclosed assets.

The carnage of the past few years hurt Massey Knakal like all real-estate brokerages. Its sales volume fell to $550 million in 2009, compared with $2 billion in 2007. The firm's new business also faces competition from established players like Meridian Capital Group and Holliday Fenoglio Fowler.

Mr. Massey says market timing didn't play a big role in the decision to launch the mortgage unit. "We're doing it because I think our company is ready for it," he says.

Massey Knakal's move focuses a spotlight on commercial buildings selling for between $1 million and $100 million. In the peak year of 2007, about $8 billion of buildings in this price range traded hands in New York City, according to Massey Knakal.

Massey Knakal survived the early 1990s partly because its overhead was low. There were only a handful of salaried employees and the firm leased space in a building so old it still had an elevator attendant.

Today the firm has a staff of about 125 and that additional overhead put a strain on the firm during the down years. In 2008 the firm laid off staff. "In 2009, we got our teeth kicked in," Mr. Massey says.

The number of commercial buildings being put up for sale is on the rise now in part because some banks are getting more aggressive in foreclosing on and selling off real estate. About 15% of the firm's roughly 500 listings of property for sale are lender-owned properties—compared with less than 5% a year ago, according to Mr. Massey, who estimates that figure will rise to as high as 25% by late next year.

Another factor in the increasing sales: a thawing financing market. Wall Street is slowly starting again to slice and dice mortgages and sell them to investors, although the volume being generated from the securitized-mortgage market is still far below what it was during the boom. Some insurance companies and community banks also looking for deals.

Some insurance companies and community banks are also looking for deals.

Jim Carpenter, chief lending officer for Westbury-based New York Community Bancorp, says the number of new commercial real estate and multifamily loan applications his bank is receiving is on the rise. The bank, which focuses on lending on rent-regulated apartment buildings, is on pace to exceed its 2009 total of $2.6 billion in multifamily and commercial real estate loan originations, Mr. Carpenter said.

Mr. Massey calls mortgage brokerage "an underserved market." But the business might not prove as lucrative as sales. Eastern Consolidated, one of Massey Knakal's competitors in building sales, also arranges mortgages—but it's not the company's focus.

"Mortgage lending is kind of a different animal," according to Eric Michael Anton, an executive managing director at Eastern Consolidated. "It's a lot of work, it's a commodity [and] you don't really get great fees on it."

Banks keep failing; no end in sight --- Since WaMu fell, 279 lenders have collapsed; lost jobs, curtailed lending and the big get bigger
By Randall Smith and Robin Sidel
971 words
28 September 2010
The Wall Street Journal Europe
WSJE
23
English
(Copyright (c) 2010, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

The largest number of bank failures in nearly 20 years has eliminated jobs, accelerated a drought in lending and left the industry's survivors with more power to squeeze customers.

Some 279 banks have collapsed since Sept. 25, 2008, when Washington Mutual Inc. became the biggest bank failure on record. That dwarfed the 1984 demise of Continental Illinois, which had one-seventh of WaMu's assets. The failures of the past two years shattered the pace of the prior six-year period, when only three dozen banks died.

Two more banks went down last Friday, and failures are expected to "persist for some time," according to a report issued Tuesday by Standard & Poor's. In the second quarter of this year, the Federal Deposit Insurance Corp. increased its number of problem banks by 6% to 829.

Between failures and consolidation, the number of U.S. banks could fall to 5,000 over the next decade from the current 7,932, according to an executive at investment-banking firm Keefe, Bruyette & Woods Inc.

The upside of failures is that they can represent a healthy cleansing of a sector that grew too fast, with bank assets more than doubling to $13.8 trillion in the decade that ended in 2008. Many banks that failed were opportunistic latecomers. Of the failed banks since February 2007, 75 were formed after 1999, according to SNL Financial.

Still, economists say, the contraction represents an enduring threat to capital, lending and the economy.

"When we step back and look at this financial disaster 10 years from now, the destruction of capital in our economy as a result of what we've endured will be the single greatest lasting impact on recovery and how the economy performs in the future," says Howard Headlee, president of the Utah Bankers Association.

The pain is less severe than in the Japanese banking crisis, in which banks languished for a decade despite $440 billion the government spent to assist the industry.

But, in the past two years, the whole U.S. banking system recoiled. Large banks like Countrywide Financial Corp. and Wachovia Corp. were acquired to avert failure while powerful banks including Citigroup Inc. and Bank of America Corp. were propped up by the government.

Between the failures and government assistance, Gerard Cassidy of RBC Capital Markets says, the impact to the system has been "far more severe" than the savings-and-loan crisis. Not only were government rescue measures more sweeping and more global this time, the weakness in real estate continues to constrain economic growth.

Since 2008, the industry's assets have shrunk by 4.5%.

"If you reduce the amount of assets at a bank, it means they make fewer loans, and that has a negative impact on the economy," says Richard Bove, a bank analyst at Rochdale Securities in Lutz, Fla.

From small towns like Rockford, Ill., to Miami, the banks' disappearance means not only cutbacks in lending but fewer banking choices, lower interest rates on savings accounts, and lost jobs.

The recession and collapse of the housing bubble have cut bank-industry employment by 188,000 jobs, or 8.5%, since 2007, according to FDIC data. Failures alone have cost 11,210 jobs, or 32% of the employees at failed banks, according to FIG Partners, an Atlanta investment firm that specializes in the banking industry.

For more than a year, Martin Quantz and his co-workers at the Woodstock, Ill., branch of Amcore Bank checked the FDIC's website each Friday afternoon to see if their flailing bank had gone under. Regulators seized the bank in April and turned over its 58 branches to Harris National Association.

By August, Mr. Quantz was unemployed. He now hopes reconnecting with an old contact will lead to a new bank job.

"There's a lot of pain out there, and there are a lot of people in the industry who won't go back," says Mr. Quantz, 41 years old.

The city of Clinton, Utah, may never be refunded $83,000, a portion of their cemetery-maintenance funds that wasn't insured when nearby Centennial Bank failed without a buyer.

In nearby Ogden, Utah, Weber State University lost $100,000 in scholarship money that had been pledged by Barnes Banking Co., a 119-year-old local institution that failed in January. The scholarships, to be distributed in $1,000 increments, represented one-quarter of in-state tuition, says a Weber State administrator. Earlier this month, the college restored the Barnes Banking Lecture Hall to its original name: "Room 110."

Failed bank assets are now strewn across the banking system.

The FDIC is burdened with $38 billion of remnants it is trying to sell. They range from virtually worthless mortgaged-backed securities to office decorations such as plastic Christmas trees.

The tough times follow cresting prosperity in which banks with few loan losses chased customers into hot real-estate markets. When the subprime mortgage bubble burst, failures were concentrated among mortgage lenders such as IndyMac Bank, which left $1 billion of depositors' money uninsured when it failed in 2008.

Autopsies of expired banks all point to real estate as the primary cause. A tally by SNL Financial LC found that 94% of bank failures since 2008 had either residential or commercial real estate as their largest category of delinquent loans.

KBW says their riskier construction loans were 23% of their total portfolio, compared with 7.2% for the industry as a whole. The delinquency rate of commercial real estate was 13.5%, far above the current national average of 1.7%, SNL said.

Business
Banks Keep Failing, No End in Sight; Since WaMu Fell, 279 Lenders Have Collapsed; Lost Jobs, Curtailed Lending and the Big Get Bigger
By Randall Smith And Robin Sidel
1383 words
27 September 2010
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2010 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

Not only were government rescue measures more sweeping and more global this time, the weakness in real estate continues to constrain economic growth.

Since 2008, the industry's assets have shrunk by 4.5%.

"If you reduce the amount of assets at a bank, it means they make fewer loans, and that has a negative impact on the economy," says Richard Bove, a bank analyst at Rochdale Securities in Lutz, Fla.

From small towns like Rockford, Ill., to Miami, the banks' disappearance means not only cutbacks in lending but fewer banking choices, lower interest rates on savings accounts, and lost jobs.

The recession and collapse of the housing bubble have cut bank-industry employment by 188,000 jobs, or 8.5%, since 2007, according to FDIC data. Failures alone have cost 11,210 jobs, or 32% of the employees at failed banks, according to FIG Partners, an Atlanta investment firm that specializes in the banking industry.

For more than a year, Martin Quantz and his co-workers at the Woodstock, Ill. branch of Amcore Bank checked the FDIC's website each Friday afternoon to see if their flailing bank had gone under. Regulators seized the bank in April and turned over its 58 branches to Harris National Association.

By August, Mr. Quantz was unemployed. He now hopes reconnecting with an old contact will lead to a new bank job.

"There's a lot of pain out there, and there are a lot of people in the industry who won't go back," says Mr. Quantz, 41 years old.

The city of Clinton, Utah, may never be refunded $83,000, a portion of their cemetery-maintenance funds that wasn't insured when nearby Centennial Bank failed without a buyer.

In nearby Ogden, Utah, Weber State University lost $100,000 in scholarship money that had been pledged by Barnes Banking Co., a 119-year-old local institution that failed in January. The scholarships, to be distributed in $1,000 increments, represented one quarter of in-state tuition, says a Weber State administrator. Earlier this month, the college restored the Barnes Banking Lecture Hall to its original name: "Room 110."

Failed bank assets are now strewn across the banking system.

The FDIC is burdened with $38 billion of remnants it is trying to sell. They range from virtually worthless mortgaged-backed securities to office decorations such as plastic Christmas trees.

The tough times follow cresting prosperity in which banks with few loan losses chased customers into hot real-estate markets. When the subprime mortgage bubble burst, failures were concentrated among mortgage lenders such as IndyMac Bank, which left $1 billion of depositors' money uninsured when it failed in 2008.

Various autopsies of expired banks all point to real estate as the primary cause. A tally by SNL Financial LC found that 94% of bank failures since 2008 had either residential or commercial real-estate as their largest category of delinquent loans. KBW says their riskier construction loans were 23% of their total portfolio, compared with 7.2% for the industry as a whole. The delinquency rate of commercial real estate was 13.5%, far above the current national average of 1.7%, SNL said.

The Imperial Capital Bank unit of Imperial Capital Bancorp in La Jolla, Calif., specialized in real estate. Like many other small banks, it extended beyond its home turf and made loans nationwide. The bank more than doubled its assets to $4.1 billion in the five years ended in 2008, according to an FDIC report. Then, the nine-branch bank purchased $826 million of mortgage-backed securities.

Real estate accounted for more than 95% of its loans, compared to 35% or less for its peers. The bank failed in 2009.

Some economists argue that, for all the damage, the failures' impact on the economy was muted because the largest banks that failed or came close were quickly absorbed by other institutions or helped by the government.

"I don't think enough banks have failed, or have been failing fast enough, to have a macro-economic impact," says economist Edward Yardeni.

Surviving banks have raised more than $500 billion in new capital, reducing the risks of new failures by boosting rainy-day funds.

Failure can occasionally jumpstart lending. To conserve capital, regulators often block sickly banks from making new loans. When a bank buys the assets of the failed institution, that buyer often resumes lending.

Since acquiring operations of the failed Frontier Bank in Everett, Wash., last April, Union Bank N.A. has started originating loans in Frontier's region in western Washington and Oregon. Though Union lowered interest rates on certificates of deposit, "We desire to grow our loan portfolio and are eager to find ways to make loans that make sense," says Tim Wennes, chief retail banking officer for Union Bank, a unit of San Francisco-based UnionBanCal Corp.

Such consolidation also means the biggest are getting bigger: Bank of America, J.P. Morgan Chase & Co. and Wells Fargo hold 33% of all U.S. deposits, up from 21% in 2006, according to SNL Financial. That gives them more market power to squeeze out smaller competitors.

John Squires, who was chief executive officer of Old Southern Bank when it failed in March, protests that his larger competitors in his Orlando, Fla., neighborhood all survived thanks to heavy doses of government support, which allowed them to raise capital more easily.

"Absolutely unfair—the big boys have the clout," says Mr. Squires. "Community banks are in jeopardy all over the country."

Dan Fitzpatrick contributed to this article.

U.S. News: Stalemate persists in housing market
By Nick Timiraos and Sara Murray
1015 words
24 September 2010
The Wall Street Journal Europe
WSJE
8
English
(Copyright (c) 2010, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

For months, home buyers and sellers have been stuck in an unusual standoff, with sellers reluctant to lower prices and buyers staying on the sidelines.

New data suggest that standoff eased slightly last month, as sales of existing, or previously owned, homes rose 7.6% from July, according to figures released Thursday by the National Association of Realtors.

But while the housing market may have halted a slide that began in April after federal home-buyer tax credits expired, it still faces a long recovery, and buyers remain scarce. The inventory of unsold homes fell 0.6% in August to 3.98 million, but it would still take 11.6 months at the current sales pace to clear that.

"The only way to sell a home in this environment is to drop the price," said John Burns, a housing consultant based in Irvine, Calif.

The housing numbers have prompted a debate among economists about how much further prices need to fall to resuscitate sales. Home prices are likely to fall another 2.2% this year, according to the consensus estimate of 114 economists and housing analysts surveyed this month by MacroMarkets LLC, a provider of hedging products.

The picture varies from region to region. Home prices in several Florida and Nevada markets are likely to fall at least another 5%, while parts of Texas and Oklahoma could post modest gains over the next year, said Eric Fox, vice president of statistical and economic modeling at in Santa Ana, Calif.

Nationally, prices in July fell by 0.5% on a seasonally adjusted basis, according to figures this week from the Federal Housing Finance Agency. The S&P/Case-Shiller home-price index will issue its report on July prices next week.

Consequently, sellers face a difficult decision: get off the market or cut the price. Sonja Brisson decided to get out. After listing her home for three months, she began interviewing prospective renters on Tuesday. Ms. Brisson, who is moving to live with her fiance, will lose money renting out her Seattle town home, but said it was better than competing with distressed sales going for 30% less than what she paid for her property.

Renting the home is "just a total crapshoot," she said. "Nobody saw this coming, and nobody can see the end of it."

Weak demand in the housing market comes amid other signs that the economy is improving, but at a painfully slow pace. An index of leading economic indicators, which aims to help predict where the economy is headed, rose 0.3% in August after increasing 0.1% in July, the Conference Board said Thursday.

Much of the lingering weakness is linked to sluggish improvements in the job market. Some 465,000 unemployed workers filed new claims for jobless benefits last week, up 12,000 from the week before, the Labor Department said Thursday.

Without jobs, families are still relying on tactics they employed during the recession -- such as households doubling up -- to make ends meet. That's pulling down demand for housing and, in turn, prices and construction.

"As those jobs get created, people who have been doubling up will start moving out of those homes and demand will pick up," said Patrick Newport, an IHS Global Insight economist. "As that happens -- it's going to happen very slowly -- the glut will start coming down."

The weak economy is just one reason why buyer psychology remains depressed. The housing market also faces a "shadow inventory" of four to five million potential foreclosures that have not yet come to market but could put pressure on prices once they do.

"Why do you rush out today to buy something when you think there are going to be millions more for sale soon," said Michael Feder, chief executive of real-estate data firm Radar Logic Inc. The question, he said, is "how much lower do prices have to go to attract the buyers?"

Even investors, who have been active over the past year buying homes at what they believed were big discounts, are pulling back. "They think everything will be cheaper next year," said Mr. Burns.

As prices fall, more sellers could find themselves in Patrick Minton's shoes. He's already dropped the price on his Seattle home to $400,000, which is less than what he owes. He'll already have to pay transaction costs out of pocket.

The home hasn't received any offers, and he isn't willing to cut the price anymore unless his bank agrees to a short sale. Mr. Minton, a 42-year-old software developer, listed his home in July after getting divorced and said he'd stay if the bank would let him refinance at current rates.

While 11 million borrowers are under water, or owe more than their homes are worth, another 2.5 million will join them if prices fall just another 5%, according to CoreLogic Inc., a real-estate analytics firm.

"They're between a rock and a hard place," said Glenn Kelman, chief executive of Redfin Corp., a real-estate brokerage that operates in nine states. "They'd capitulate if the bank would let them."

That is suffocating the market because those sellers are also would-be buyers. "Right now, we have investors and first-timers in control of the market, and until that changes, we will never be on the mend," said Mark Hanson, an independent housing analyst in Menlo Park, Calif.

The housing market will eventually need more buyers like Robert Gifford, who spent six months with his wife scouring their Beacon Hill neighborhood in Seattle before pulling the trigger on a sale in July.

When it came time to sell their smaller home this month, they didn't dawdle. They cut the listing price by around 10% to $334,000 earlier this month, and it quickly went to contract. "We didn't want to become an involuntary landlord," said the 31-year-old engineer.

Dow Jones & Company, Inc.

U.S. News: Housing: Stuck and Staying Stuck --- Inventories of Unsold Homes Are Swollen, but Anticipation of Further Price Drops Makes Buyers Scarce
By Nick Timiraos and Sara Murray
1071 words
24 September 2010
The Wall Street Journal

While 11 million borrowers are underwater, or owe more than their homes are worth, another 2.5 million will join them if prices decline just another 5%, according to CoreLogic Inc., a real-estate analytics firm.

"They're between a rock and a hard place," said Glenn Kelman, chief executive of Redfin Corp., a real-estate brokerage that operates in nine states. "They'd capitulate if the bank would let them."

That is suffocating the market because those sellers are also would-be buyers. "Right now, we have investors and first-timers in control of the market, and until that changes, we will never be on the mend," said Mark Hanson, an independent housing analyst in Menlo Park, Calif.

The housing market will eventually need more buyers like Robert Gifford, who spent six months with his wife scouring their Beacon Hill neighborhood in southeast Seattle before pulling the trigger on a sale in July.

When it came time to sell their smaller home this month, they didn't dawdle. They cut the listing price by around 10% to $334,000, and it quickly went to contract.

"We didn't want to become an involuntary landlord," said the 31-year-old engineer.

European Stock Markets
European markets slip; Dow ends with tiny gain
By Andrea Tryphonides
583 words
22 September 2010
The Wall Street Journal Europe
WSJE
27
English
(Copyright (c) 2010, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

European stocks shed modest early gains, ending in the red Tuesday as investors waited for news from a meeting of the Federal Reserve committee in charge of monetary policy.

The losses came despite successful bond auctions by some of the countries embroiled most deeply in the European sovereign-debt crisis.

The Stoxx Europe 600 index ended down 0.5% at 265.01. The U.K.'s FTSE 100 closed down 0.5% at 5576.19, Germany's DAX fell 0.3% to 6275.98 and France's CAC-40 closed 0.1% lower at 3784.40. With many of the main European indexes surpassing technically key levels this week, traders said that any upside would undoubtedly be capped.

The Dow Jones Industrial Average closed up 7.41 points, or 0.1%, at 10761.03 after the Federal Reserve held rates steady and said inflation levels remained below its comfort zone, hinting at the possibility of further quantitative easing.

Market sentiment improved a touch during the first part of the session as Ireland successfully offered 500 million euros ($653 million) of 2014 bonds and 1 billion euros of 2018 bonds. Also Treasury bill auctions raised 7.04 billion euros for Spain and 390 million euros for Greece.

These countries have been at the epicenter of the European sovereign-debt crisis. Last week, Ireland was forced to deny it is going to appeal to the International Monetary Fund for financial assistance. And just four months ago, Athens was frozen out of the sovereign bond market when it came perilously close to default, but was rescued by the European Union and the IMF.

The auction results bode well for an auction of Portuguese debt on Wednesday.

Meanwhile, U.S. housing starts for August surprised investors, rising 10.5% in the month. The outcome was stronger than expected and marked the first positive year-on-year reading since May, said Annalisa Piazza, economist at Newedge Strategy. Ms. Piazza warned not to expect a reacceleration in housing activity. "Sideways movements are the most likely scenario in the coming months," she said.

Despite the positive data, there was a sense overall of markets treading water ahead of the Federal Open Market Committee rate announcement and subsequent comments.

Although many market participants didn't expect the Federal Reserve to expand its quantitative-easing measures or change interest rates, most were keen to hear the rhetoric of the committee, to see whether the potential for a slowdown in growth has increased since the last meeting. The Federal Open Market Committee's announcement came after the close of European financial markets.

In the foreign-exchange markets Tuesday, the euro was at $1.3244 from $1.3062 late Monday. The dollar was at 85.12 yen from 85.75 yen, while the euro was at 112.75 yen from 112.02 yen. The U.K. pound was at $1.5613 from $1.5552. The dollar was at 0.9972 Swiss franc from 1.0050 francs.

In major market action: Banks were in focus once again, with shares in BNP Paribas up 1% in Paris following reports that its chief executive said the company won't need to raise more capital.

Natixis fell 4.7% after Goldman cut its rating to "sell" from "neutral." Cairn Energy added 2.3% after saying it found oil in Greenland.

The Property Report: Banks get creative with refinancing --- Mezzanine-debt lenders like LaSalle Investment see an opportunity to step in to fill a gap and reap hefty profits
By William Boston Special to The WSJ
942 words
22 September 2010
The Wall Street Journal Europe
WSJE
24
English
(Copyright (c) 2010, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

Investors and lenders are getting creative as Europe's lingering credit squeeze puts pressure on banks to refinance a mountain of debt over the next few years.

One sign of this: Germany's Eurohypo AG has taken control of an office development at 6 Bevis Marks in London from Spanish developer Monteverde and is now in talks to build on the site. Rather than sell the development outright, and be forced to book the losses, Eurohypo is retaining a link to the property by forming a joint venture that will allow it to share in any future profits from the project.

In another example of investor creativity, mezzanine-debt lenders are beginning to re-emerge. The latest example of the trend involves LaSalle Investment Management, a unit of property-services group Jones Lang LaSalle with $40 billion of assets under management. It has raised GBP 100 million ($155.5 million) for a U.K. Special Situations fund.

Amy Aznar, head of special situations at LaSalle Investment Management, says the fund is negotiating with several major banks to provide mezzanine financing to help the lenders work with borrowers to restructure loans on a number of central London office properties. She declined to identify them. "About GBP 200 billion of debt is coming due in the U.K. over the next four to five years, and it either needs to be extended or to be refinanced," says Ms. Aznar. "We are dealing with a number of banks directly, and we are seeing a lot of borrowers come to us for refinancing."

Mezzanine financing is typically provided by a lender in exchange for rights to convert the debt to equity in the case of nonpayment. It is becoming increasingly attractive because as European property markets slowly recover, the squeeze on debt financing shows no signs of easing. That is frustrating efforts of private-equity investors to make acquisitions while prices are still low.

Many a deal over the past year has failed to be completed because of what investors call the "debt gap," the difference between what banks are willing to lend and what an investor needs to ensure that an acquisition generates the returns required by private-equity funds.

Banks are under pressure to refinance about GBP 50 billion of debt a year for the next four to five years, and new Basel III capital rules that will require banks to maintain 7% core capital are forcing banks to strengthen their balance sheets. The situation is creating an opportunity for mezzanine lenders to step in to fill the debt gap, and possibly reap hefty profits for their services.

Ms. Aznar says LaSalle's offering won't be limited to mezzanine financing because there is also a growing need for lending to help finance new developments and acquisitions.

"This is not just for refinancing, but also for new purchases," she says. "A lot of private-equity buyers can't get 80% to 85% loan-to-value today, and that makes it hard to make a 20% internal rate of return."

Between 2005 and 2007, about GBP 232 billion of new debt was lent to the property sector in the U.K., according to a survey of the industry by De Montfort University. The majority of the U.K. loans were made by a handful of large lenders, such as Royal Bank of Scotland Group PLC, Lloyds Banking Group PLC, Eurohypo and Hypo Real Estate. RBS and Lloyds received huge bailouts by the British government during the crisis. Hypo Real Estate was nationalized by the German government and renamed Deutsche Pfandbriefbank.

The banks are now moving to reduce the size of their balance sheets and weed out nonperforming loans, creating opportunities for debt investors.

In August, RBS sold a 1.4 billion euro ($1.83 billion) portfolio of European senior loans backed by a diverse range of properties, to Intermediate Capital Group PLC, a private-equity investor that specializes in mezzanine finance. The portfolio represented about 10% of RBS's leveraged loan portfolio at the end of June.

In light of sluggishness among European property stocks, some investors are turning to debt as a way to achieve better returns. Executives at a number of major institutions, including APG Asset Management, the Dutch pension-fund manager with 200 billion euros of assets under management, have said in recent weeks that mezzanine financing provided an opportunity for taking advantage of market distress.

The Eurohypo's 6 Bevis Marks deal, meanwhile, is a typical way that banks are trying to work out distressed loans without being forced to book any losses. Eurohypo did a similar deal recently with U.K. real-estate investment trust Great Portland Estates PLC. That joint venture was set up to complete construction of a London West End office development called Marcol House.

Investors believe such opportunities are bound to increase over the coming year as banks continue to shrink their balance sheets.

"There are still big liquidity issues," says Jos Short, chairman and founding partner of Internos Real Investors, a U.K.-based REIT with 1.5 billion euros under management. "Over the next 12 to 24 months, there will be more unbundling [of banks' real-estate assets] than we've seen so far since the crisis."

The 6 Bevis Marks development could be partly financed by French insurance group AXA SA, according to people that are familiar with the situation.

World News: U.S.: Housing starts jump unexpectedly
By Jeff Bater and Jeffrey Sparshott Dow Jones Newswires
471 words
22 September 2010
The Wall Street Journal Asia
AWSJ
8
English
(c) 2010 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. To see the edition in which this article appeared, click here http://awsj.com.hk/factiva-ns[http://awsj.com.hk/factiva-ns]

U.S. housing starts surged a better-than-expected 10.5% in August to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 598,000 on strength in multifamily construction, the Commerce Department said Tuesday.

Economists surveyed by had expected overall housing starts to fall 0.2% to 545,000 during August, largely because buyers have remained concerned about a weak job market and uncertain economic prospects.

August's figures indicate a strong month-on-monthrise even after the April 30 expiration of tax incentives for first-time purchases, and a 2.2% rise from the same period a year earlier.

Single-family housing starts rose in August by 4.3% to an annual rate of 438,000. Groundbreakings on homes with two or more units in August surged 32.2% to 160,000.

Building permits increased 1.8% to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 569,000.

The Commerce Department's numbers also reflect a downward revision of July's figures to 541,000 starts from 546,000.

The sharp increase in August starts was a surprise. The housing market tumbled after a government tax incentive for some purchases ended April 30. While mortgage rates are low, the job market is weak, discouraging would-be buyers. The builders that are willing to overlook lousy sales and break ground face problems securing credit as the economy slowly recovers from a financial crisis and a recession that lasted 18 months, the longest the country has endured since the Great Depression.

Home builder Lennar Corp. said Monday that its sales pace fell in the third quarter because the tax credit ended. The profit that the company reported was helped by finance-related activities largely unrelated to the core home-building business. Chief Executive Stuart Miller noted that "high unemployment and foreclosures have continued to present challenges for the national housing market."

A housing-industry group this week released results of a survey of home builders showing a gauge of sales expectations over the next six months remained unchanged at a very low level, while a gauge of traffic of prospective buyers edged down. "Fewer shoppers are walking through showroom doors -- a sign that Americans by and large just aren't that excited about the prospect of buying a house," said Mike Larson, an analyst at Weiss Research.

Regionally, housing starts in August increased 7.0% in the South, 34.3% in the West, and 21.7% in the Midwest. Starts fell 24.3% in the Northeast.

The Commerce Department said actual housing starts rose to 54,800 in August from 50,600 in July. Lumber and commodities markets watch those numbers closely to gauge demand.

The Property Report: Insurers' cash: A prop for property --- China's decision to let firms invest in real estate could create billions of dollars in demand; a push for quality
By Aaron Back
902 words
22 September 2010
The Wall Street Journal Asia
AWSJ
12
English
(c) 2010 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. To see the edition in which this article appeared, click here http://awsj.com.hk/factiva-ns[http://awsj.com.hk/factiva-ns]

BEIJING -- China is set to see a wave of demand for quality commercial real estate as insurance companies are allowed for the first time to invest in the sector, according to Colin Dyer, the chief executive officer of real-estate consultancy Jones Lang LaSalle Inc.

In August, China's insurance regulator announced a long-awaited revision to its rules, allowing insurance companies to invest as much as 10% of their assets in commercial real estate and put up to 20% of their assets into the stock market.

Analysts are concerned that restrictions put in place this year on home purchases will become a drag on overall economic growth as construction and investment activity in that sector slows. New demand for commercial real estate from insurers could therefore become an important support for the property sector and the wider Chinese economy.

China's insurance companies have around 4.6 trillion yuan ($685.15 billion) of assets under management, growing at around 20% per year, Mr. Dyer said in an interview on Monday. If they invest their full 10% allotment into commercial real estate, that amounts to 460 billion yuan, or $68.5 billion of potential demand, compared with just $17 billion of total investment last year in commercial real estate, he said.

"So you've got a massive potential pulse of investment money looking for real estate in China," Mr. Dyer said. "It will promote development work to meet that investment demand."

Insurance companies invest for the long term and want to derive as much as 20 years of cash flow from a property, which will drive demand for quality, "investment grade" properties, Mr. Dyer said.

That would mark a sea change in China, where developers often follow a "build-and-sell" model of unloading a property with little regard for its long-term viability, which has negative consequences for building quality and management practices, he added.

The real-estate sector accounts for around 10% of China's gross domestic product, giving rise to concerns that if there is a downturn in the sector it could drag the rest of the economy down.

Analysts say a renewed drive by Beijing to encourage the construction of more affordable housing will be an important support measure. New demand for commercial property could help as well, although its importance is limited, as around 70% of the total real-estate market in China is residential, Jones Lang LaSalle estimates.

Not everyone is enthusiastic about the prospect of large-scale investment by insurance companies in China's property market. In a recent report, Moody's Corp. warned that the risks of investing in property may outweigh the benefits and could hurt the creditworthiness of Chinese insurers.

Commercial real estate in China is characterized by high vacancy rates, and prices have risen strongly in the past year, the credit-ratings company said. "We are wary of insurers purchasing real-estate assets at their high point," it said.

In China's top-tier cities, vacancy rates are high by international standards. Jones Lang LaSalle estimates that 22.8% of Beijing's premium "Grade A" office space was vacant at the end of June. But it also estimates that since the start of the year, the vacancy rate has fallen by 5.3 percentage points and office rents have risen by 7.3%. In Shanghai, 11.5% of Grade-A office space in the central business districts was vacant, unchanged from the start of the year, but rents are up 6.6% year to date.

China's high growth rate helps explain the disparity between high vacancy rates and rising rents, Mr. Dyer said.

"In the context of China's astronomical growth rate, a 20% vacancy rate, which would be a big problem in a Western city in a mature economy growing at 2%-3%, is just a year-and-a-half supply in China," he said.

Similarly, Mr. Dyer isn't very concerned about the outlook for China's residential market, despite widespread concerns that home prices are too high, especially in major cities. Jones Lang LaSalle is less directly involved in that market than in the commercial real-estate market, but based on what the company hears from market participants, Mr. Dyer believes underlying demand for homes remains strong in China.

The Chinese government has instituted a series of measures since April to cool the residential property market, including higher down payment requirements for mortgages. "Although that might control pricing for a while, it's unlikely to reduce the absolute level of demand and transaction levels, simply because the demand is so strong," Mr. Dyer said.

Property prices in 70 Chinese cities were flat for the second consecutive month in August, but up 9.3% from a year earlier, according to data released by the National Bureau of Statistics.

In terms of floor space, nationwide property sales fell 10.1% to 68.9 million square meters in August from the same month a year earlier, but were higher than July's 64.7 million square meters. The rebound came one month ahead of the usual peak month for property sales in September.

The Recession and the Housing Drag
By Mortimer Zuckerman
1029 words
22 September 2010
The Wall Street Journal Asia
AWSJ
17
English
(c) 2010 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. To see the edition in which this article appeared, click here http://awsj.com.hk/factiva-ns[http://awsj.com.hk/factiva-ns]

A decent house has long been a symbol of middle-class American family life. Practically, it has been a secure shelter for the children and provided access to a good public education. And financially, it has been regarded as a safe store of value, a shield against the vagaries of the economy and a long-term retirement asset.

All that seems a distant memory for the millions of American families who must confront the decline in the value of their homes. The pressure to meet mortgage payments on properties that have lost value has been especially shocking for those who have lost their jobs in the Great Recession. Their houses have become a ball and chain, restricting their ability to seek employment elsewhere. They cannot afford to abandon the remaining equity they have in their houses -- and they can't sell in this miserable market.

New home sales, pending home sales, and mortgage applications are down to a 13-year low, despite long-term mortgage rates that plummeted recently to an average 4.3% before rising slightly. New home prices have fallen by an average of 30%. According to David Rosenberg, chief economist at Gluskin Sheff, this has reduced home occupancy cost to 15% of family incomes, down from the conventional 25%.

The fall in house prices has eaten away at the equity Americans have in their homes. About 11 million residential properties have mortgage balances that exceed the home's value, notes Mr. Rosenberg. And given the total inventory of homes and the shadow inventory of 3.7 million empty (foreclosed) homes, he notes that prices might fall another 5% to 10%. That would leave an estimated 40% of American homeowners with mortgages in excess of the value of their homes.

Disappearing equity invites strategic defaults. Homeowners with negative equity are tempted simply to mail in their keys to their friendly lender even if they can afford the mortgage payment. Yet banks don't want to take the deflated properties onto their books because they will then have to declare a financial loss and still have to worry about maintaining the properties. Little wonder, according to Mr. Rosenberg, that foreclosure has not been enforced on a quarter of the people who haven't made a single mortgage payment in the last two years.

A staggering eight million home loans are in some state of delinquency, default or foreclosure, Alan Abelson reported in Barron's in July. He noted that another eight million homeowners are estimated to have mortgages representing 95% or more of the value of their homes, leaving them with 5% or less equity in their homes and thus vulnerable to further price declines.

The pace of foreclosures slowed briefly thanks to loan modifications by the Home Affordable Modification Program and other government efforts. But the programs have not been working as hoped -- it's been reported that half of the borrowers have been redefaulting within 12 months, even after monthly payments were cut by as much as 50%.

While the foreclosure pipeline remains clogged, as it unclogs a new wave of homes will wash into the market and precipitate additional downward pressure on prices. The number of foreclosed homes put on the market by banks will be a more powerful influence on the further decline of home prices than either consumer demand or interest rates.

A well-balanced housing market has a supply of about five to six months. These days the inventory backlog has surged to about a 12 1/2 months' supply. This explains why average sale prices have been declining for so many months. The high end of the market, in particular, is under great pressure.

The mortgage market is also deeply troubled. Conventional lenders now insist on a substantial down payment and impose other more stringent financial requirements. Household formation (marriages) is also shrinking now, down to an annual rate of about 600,000, compared to net household formation in excess of a million annually during the bubble years.

The most critical factor subduing the demand for housing is that home ownership is no longer seen as the great, long-term buildup in equity value. So it is not too difficult to understand why demand for housing has declined and will not revive anytime soon.

This is a disturbing development for those who believe that housing is going to lead America to an economic recovery, as it did during the Great Depression and every recession since. In the past, residential construction preceded the recovery in the larger economy. This time a lead weight on recovery has been the disappearance of some $6 trillion of home equity value, a loss that has had a devastating effect on consumer confidence, retirement savings and current spending.

Every further 1% decline in home prices today lowers household wealth by approximately $170 billion, according to Goldman Sachs. For each dollar lost in housing wealth, Goldman Sachs estimates that consumption is lowered by 5 cents. Add to this the fact that we are building a million-plus fewer homes on an annual basis from the peak years of the housing boom. With five people or more working on each home, we have permanently lost over five million jobs in residential construction.

That is why housing was such an important generator of normal economic recoveries. To give this context, residential construction was 6.3% of GDP at its recent peak in 2005 and 2006 but has fallen to 2.4% this year, according to economist A. Gary Shilling. This is significant if you recognize that a 3% top-to-bottom decline in real GDP constitutes a serious recession.

There is no painless, quick fix for this catastrophe. The more the government tries to paper over the housing crisis and prevent housing from seeking its own equilibrium value in real terms, the longer it will take to find out what is true market pricing and then be able to grow from there.

---

Mr. Zuckerman is chairman and editor in chief of U.S. News & World Report.

Dow Jones & Company, Inc.

Business at a Glance
Business Watch
1405 words
20 September 2010
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English

Autos

Toyota made an undisclosed settlement with the relatives of a California Highway patrolman who was killed along with three passengers in 2009 accident believed to have been caused by a floor mat that became lodged against the accelerator pedal. Toyota still faces many other lawsuits, including one in federal court that was filed by hundreds of people who claim their Toyota's accelerated out of control and blaming the electronic throttle control system, a charge that Toyota has denied.[http://online.wsj.com/article/SB40001424052748703556604575501693805105922.html]

Fiat hired Toyota executive Andrea Formica to head its namesake car brand, succeeding Lorenzo Sistino, who will oversee the Italian car maker's expansion in emerging markets. Formica, 49, will oversee sales for not only Fiat but also the group's two other brands: Lancia and Alfa Romeo. In addition, he will be responsible for Chrysler's sales in Europe.

***

Aviation

The merger of UAL's United Airlines and Continental Airlines cleared its last big hurdle when shareholders of both companies approved the deal by a wide margin. The transaction will create the world's largest airline by traffic, vaulting ahead of Delta Air Lines for the top spot.[http://online.wsj.com/article/SB40001424052748703904304575497790197785582.html]

***

Banking

The U.K. government's Independent Commission on Banking is set to pay close attention to competition in the sector and could potentially recommend a split-up of large retail banking operations, a person familiar with the situation said. The commission, set up in June following the near collapse of the banking system in the U.K. during the financial crisis, will publish an issues paper Friday detailing the scope of its investigations.[http://online.wsj.com/article/SB40001424052748703556604575501592922991222.html]

U.S. regulators seized six banks in Georgia, New Jersey, Ohio and Wisconsin, marking 125 failures for 2010. The estimated loss to the FDIC's insurance fund for the six failures is $347.6 million.[http://online.wsj.com/article/SB40001424052748703904304575498451652016186.html]

Citigroup is selling its 80% stake in Student Loan Corp. to Discover Financial Services as part of a $600 million transaction that marks the bank's biggest step yet to shed assets from its discard pile. The sale of $32 billion in loan assets will help reduce the size of Citi Holdings, a trove of unprofitable noncore assets that once topped 40% of Citi's balance sheet. Once the transaction is completed, Citi Holdings will represent less than 20% of the bank's $1.9 trillion in assets by year-end.[http://online.wsj.com/article/SB40001424052748703904304575497812285217790.html]

J.P. Morgan Chase CEO James Dimon questioned whether the Federal Reserve Bank of New York's implementation of a provision of the Dodd-Frank Wall Street reform law will allow him to perform his duties on the Fed board. Dimon and other bank representatives on the board could end up resigning, though the issue remains unresolved, said people familiar with the matter.[http://online.wsj.com/article/SB40001424052748704858304575498252800975496.html]

HSBC is selling a controlling interest in its Asian private-equity business in a management buyout that values the business at $18.8 million. The move comes as banks around the world face regulatory changes that could include limits in ownership of hedge and private-equity funds or an increase in costs for holding such assets.

Royal Bank of Scotland has signed an agreement with China's Guolian Securities to form a venture that will manage equity sales in China, a person familiar with the matter said. The deal comes amid RBS's efforts to raise earnings to repay the bailout it received from the U.K. government.

Energy

Royal Bank of Scotland and Sempra Energy are nearing a deal to sell their joint venture's North American electricity retail business to Hong Kong-based commodities trading company Noble Group, people familiar with the situation said.

Entertainment

Sahara India Pariwar, an Indian conglomerate, is in talks to acquire beleaguered Metro-Goldwyn-Mayer for more than $2 billion, according to a person familiar with the matter. The discussions come as creditors prepare to take over MGM in a prepackaged bankruptcy next month and turn management over to Spyglass Entertainment co-heads Gary Barber and Roger Birnbaum.[http://online.wsj.com/article/SB40001424052748703556604575501971212232114.html]

Financial services

Citadel Investment Group is in preliminary talks with a few investors on reducing management fees and allowing easier redemptions for its two flagship funds, a person familiar with the situation said. The talks on fees and redemptions came despite continued improvement in the funds' performance. The two funds—Citadel Kensington Global Strategies and Citadel Wellington—rose almost 4% through mid-September this year, after recording a 62% surge last year, the person said.[http://online.wsj.com/article/SB40001424052748703904304575497952311727726.html]

Brit Insurance Holdings, a U.K. insurer and reinsurer, said a consortium formed by New York-based Apollo Global Management and CVC Capital Partners has submitted an indicative proposal to buy Brit at a price of £10.75 ($16.8) per share in cash.

3i Group said that Jonathan Russell, one of its star dealmakers, is quitting the firm amid an organizational shakeup intended to position the business to grow following changes in the private-equity industry. Russell, 50, is currently managing partner of buyouts. He has been with 3i for 24 years, a member of the group's management committee and investment committee since 1999.

Media

Two of Tribune's key creditors proposed their own blueprint for easing the media company out of bankruptcy protection, in an effort to help unblock Tribune's rocky efforts to exit bankruptcy after nearly two years.[http://online.wsj.com/article/SB40001424052748703904304575498614110490220.html]

Pharmaceuticals

Johnson & Johnson said it is in advanced talks to buy Crucell for €1.75 billion ($2.3 billion), a move that would make the Dutch company the core of the U.S. drug maker's vaccine business. J&J already owns 17.9% of the biotechnology firm, but wants full control so it can develop and commercialize Crucell's pipeline of vaccines.[http://online.wsj.com/article/SB40001424052748703904304575497053542363616.html]

Colleen Goggins, the head of Johnson & Johnson's consumer unit, will retire next year, after a string of manufacturing problems prompted a massive recall of children's Tylenol, causing hundreds of millions of dollars in lost sales.[http://online.wsj.com/article/SB40001424052748703440604575496140343857022.html]

Elan, the Irish biotech company locked in a spat with some of its shareholders and directors, said two critics on the board have decided to resign. The directors, Jack Schuler and Vaughn Bryson, had attempted to commission an audit of management and board practices, on concern about potential conflicts of interest involving the sale of Elan assets and poor transparency related to the company's partnerships with other drug companies.[http://online.wsj.com/article/SB40001424052748704858304575498044040286722.html]

Property

Office vacancies in Dubai's financial center could hit 40% over the next couple of years as oversupply chokes the emirate's already battered property market, a new report from real-estate consultancy Jones Lang LaSalle said.

China Vanke, China's largest property developer by market share, has allowed a plan to raise 11.2 billion yuan ($1.7 billion) via a public share offering to lapse after failing to secure regulatory approval for the deal. China's property developers face stricter controls since Beijing introduced a series of measures to curb excessively high rises in home prices.[http://online.wsj.com/article/SB40001424052748703904304575497471754170384.html]

Several U.S. technology firms, including Google, Apple, Intel, Adobe, Intuit and Walt Disney's Pixar unit, are in advanced talks with the Justice Department to avoid a court battle over whether they colluded to hold down wages by agreeing not to poach each other's employees, according to people familiar with the matter.[http://online.wsj.com/article/SB40001424052748703440604575496182527552678.html]

Yahoo previewed updates to several advertising and consumer products, including a revamp of its email service and additional features to its search engine, but few new products were unveiled.[http://online.wsj.com/article/SB40001424052748703440604575496102709839586.html]

Samsung Electronics Chairman Lee Kun-hee signaled that the company's component businesses have reached a peak, suggesting that profit may fall next year from this year's record level.[http://online.wsj.com/article/SB40001424052748704416904575501512928510660.html]

Sharp's president lambasted the Japanese government for failing to create a better environment for "fair international competition," saying the yen's strength and Japan's corporate-tax rate are putting the electronics maker at a great disadvantage against its South Korean competitors.[http://online.wsj.com/article/SB40001424052748703904304575497193040329172.html]

LG Electronics' chief executive, Nam Yong, resigned amid difficulties reshaping its cellphone business for smartphones. He will be succeeded by Koo Bon-joon, a member of the founding family of the broader LG conglomerate, the company said.[http://online.wsj.com/article/SB40001424052748703440604575496671024649064.html]

Microsoft said Windows Phone 7, a long-awaited operating system for smartphones, initially will work only with cellular networks based on the GSM technology.[http://online.wsj.com/article/SB40001424052748703440604575496721181043604.html]

Italian telecommunications operators have reached an agreement on technical aspects to build a high-speed broadband network, paving the way to a final deal for the creation of a countrywide fiber-optic infrastructure.[http://online.wsj.com/article/SB40001424052748703470904575499432808468868.html]
